Then as for the fourth figure: the syllogism can also produce a true conclusion even if the premises are false. Either in affirmation: [1] when each of the two premises is false universally, as in our statement: Every stone is an animal and every human is a stone. [2] When each of them is false partially, as in our statement: Every animal is a human and every body is an animal. [3] When the major premise is affirmative particular, as in our statement in the first example: Some human is a stone, and in the second: Some rational is an animal. As for when the minor is universal negative and the major universal affirmative: [4] if each of them is false universally, as in: No rational is a human and every horse is rational; [5] if they are false partially, as in: No stone is white and every black is a stone. As for when the minor is affirmative and the major negative: [6] if the affirmative is universal and each of them is false universally, as in: Every inanimate is a human and no stone is inanimate; [7] if they are false partially, as in: Every existent is an animal and no human is an existent. [8] If the affirmative is particular and the major is false universally or partially, then from the previous examples mentioned, the state of each can be known; so there is no need for explanation. And God is the Helper.

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of cases where a fourth-figure syllogism produces a true conclusion despite false premises. The author systematically covers combinations of universal/particular premises with affirmative/negative quality, distinguishing between premises that are false in whole (kādhibatān fī al-kull) or in part (kādhibatān fī al-baʿḍ). The examples use standard terms like 'stone', 'animal', 'human', 'rational', 'horse', and 'existent' to illustrate the relation of the middle term. The key insight is that truth of conclusion does not require truth of premises; rather, it depends on the formal structure. In Islamic legal reasoning, a similar point applies: a qiyās may yield a correct ruling even if one premise is weak, as long as the logical form is sound.

Classifying Mutually Opposite Premises
Classification of opposite premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of verbal opposites and distinguish the three that constitute real logical opposition.

Core Idea
Only three of the four verbal opposite pairs are true logical opposites.

In this section, the author introduces the concept of composing syllogisms from mutually opposite premises. He first classifies the verbal opposites into four pairs, then explains that only three are real opposites. Understanding this classification is essential for recognizing valid and deceptive syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
List the four verbal opposite pairs
The author lists: 'every, not every', 'every, not one', 'some, not one', and 'some, not some'. Write them down.
2
Identify which pair is not a real opposite
The author says 'some, not some' has no opposition. Why? Because 'some S is P' and 'some S is not P' can both be true simultaneously; they are not contradictory or contrary.
In categorical logic, subcontraries: both can be true.
3
Explain why the other three are real opposites
The first three pairs are genuine contraries or contradictories: 'every, not every' (contradictory), 'every, not one' (contrary), 'some, not one' (contradictory). Confirm with the standard square of opposition.
The author uses his own terminology.
4
Check the significance for syllogisms
Only real opposites can be used in syllogisms that produce a necessary conclusion. The false pair would not yield a valid inference.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في أن القياس قد يؤلف من مقدمات متقابلة) والقياس قد يؤلف من مقدمات متقابلة؛ وذلك بأن يؤلف من موجبة وسالبة متقابلتين نحو المضادتين وغيرهما احتيالا وتغليطًا على المخاطب. وكثيرًا ما يستعمل هذا القياس في الجدل على سبيل المبالغة في الإلزام، والمتقابلات في اللفظ أربعة : كل ، ولا كل» ، «كل ، ولا واحد»، «بعض، ولا واحد»، «بعض، ولا بعض». وفي الحقيقية ثلاثة لأن البعض ، ولا بعض من جملة ما لا تقابل فيه. وأما اشتراك المقدمتين في الحدود فذلك من اللوازم وإلا لا يلزم من تركيب القياس منهما سلب الشيء عن نفسه. وإنما يخفى عليه ذلك بأن يعبر اللفظ في المقدمة ويورد بدل اسم الحد ما يراد فيه ويحكم عليه بحكم تقابل ما حكم به على الحد في المقدمة الأولى كما في قولنا كل إنسان شر ولا شيء من الشر بآدمي لينتج لا شيء من الإنسان بآدمي، أو يورد بدل الحد كلية أو جزئية ويحكم عليه برفع الحكم عن الحد: أما الأول فكقولنا كل إنسان ناطق ولا شيء من الحيوان بناطق؛ فإن الحكم على الحيوان حكم على الناطق لما أن الناطق بحسب الحيوان، والحكم على الكلي حكم على الجزئي الذي تحته؛ وحينئذ يلزم سلب الناطق عن الإنسان. وأما الثاني فكقولنا كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الضاحك بالفعل بحيوان فينتج لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل ؛ ويلزم منه أيضًا رفع الحيوانية عن الإنسان لما أن الضاحك بالفعل مشتمل على الحيوانية. ثم إنه إنما يتألف في الشكل الأول أن تكون الحدود مترادفة حتى إذا كرر الوسط بلفظ واحد كانت الكبرى مقابلة للصغرى فجعل المحمول في الصغرى كشيئين؛ وقد وضع أحدهما للآخر إيجابًا في الصغرى وسلبًا في الكبرى كما في المثال الذي مرَّ ذكره. وأما في الشكل الثاني فالترادف بين الأصغر والأكبر لازم لأنه جعل فيه الشيء الواحد كشيئين وحمل عليهما محمول واحد. وكذلك في الشكل الثالث لأنه جعل الشيء الواحد كشيئين وحمل كل واحد منهما على موضوع واحد مثال الشكل الثاني قد مرَّ من قبل، ومثال الشكل الثالث قولنا كل طب علم ولا طب بعلم فليس كل علم بعلم. ثم إنه إذا كان مؤلفًا من المتضادتين في الشكل الثاني يصح أن تكون كل واحدة منهما الصغرى والكبرى. فأما إذا كان مؤلفًا من المتناقضتين فلا يصح إلا وأن تكون الكبرى نفي الكلية منهما موجبة كانت أو سالبة، ويصح أن يكون أحد الطرفين فيه أخص من الآخر كما إذا قلت إن كل علم فاضل ولا شيء من العلوم بفاضل فيكون هذا قياسا من مقدمتين متقابلتين. ثمَّ إنك إذا وضعت بدل العلم الموضوع في أحد الطرفين الطب كان القياس أيضًا من متقابلتين مع أن أحد طرفين أخص من الآخر. وأما في الشكل الثالث فلا يمكن أن يكون القياس فيه مؤلفًا من المتقابلتين ويكون الطرف الأكبر أخص من الأصغر وينتج غير الحق. فإنا إذا قلنا كل هندسة علم ولا شيء من الهندسة بطب ينتج ليس كل علم بطب؛ وهو حق.
Section: On that the syllogism may be composed of mutually opposite premises. The syllogism may be composed of mutually opposite premises; that is, by composing from an affirmative and a negative that are opposites, such as contraries and others, as a stratagem and deception against the interlocutor. And this syllogism is often used in dialectic for the purpose of exaggeration in obligation. The verbal opposites are four: 'every, not every', 'every, not one', 'some, not one', 'some, not some'. And in reality they are three, because 'some, not some' is among what has no opposition. As for the two premises sharing the terms, that is among the necessary conditions; otherwise, composing a syllogism from them does not necessitate the negation of a thing about itself. And it is hidden from him (the opponent) only by substituting in the premise a word for the name of the term that is meant and judging it with the judgment of the opposite of what was judged about the term in the first premise, as in our saying: 'Every human is evil' and 'Nothing evil is Adamic' to yield 'No human is Adamic'. Or he substitutes for the term a universal or particular and judges it with the lifting of the judgment about the term: as for the first, it is like our saying: 'Every human is rational' and 'No animal is rational', for the judgment about animal is a judgment about rational because rational pertains to animal, and the judgment about the universal is a judgment about the particular under it; then the negation of rational about human follows. As for the second, it is like our saying: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Nothing that actually laughs is an animal' to yield 'No human is an actual laugher'; and from it also follows the removal of animality from human because the actual laugher includes animality. Then it is composed in the first figure only when the terms are synonymous, such that when the middle is repeated with one expression, the major premise is opposite to the minor, making the predicate in the minor as two things; and one of them is placed for the other affirmatively in the minor and negatively in the major, as in the example that passed. As for the second figure, synonymy between the minor and major terms is necessary because it makes one thing into two things and predicates a single predicate on them. Similarly in the third figure because it makes one thing into two things and predicates each of them on a single subject. An example of the second figure has passed earlier, and an example of the third figure is our saying: 'Every medicine is knowledge' and 'No medicine is knowledge' so 'Not every knowledge is knowledge'. Then if it is composed from contraries in the second figure, it is valid for either of them to be the minor or the major. But if it is composed from contradictories, it is not valid unless the major premise is the negation of the universal of them, whether affirmative or negative, and it is valid that one of the two terms is more specific than the other, as when you say: 'Every knowledge is virtuous' and 'Nothing of the sciences is virtuous' — this would be a syllogism from two opposite premises. Then if you substitute for 'science' the subject in one of the two terms with 'medicine', the syllogism is also from two opposites, with one term more specific than the other. As for the third figure, it is not possible for the syllogism therein to be composed from two opposites and for the major term to be more specific than the minor and produce a false conclusion. For if we say: 'Every geometry is knowledge' and 'Nothing of geometry is medicine' it yields 'Not every knowledge is medicine', and it is true.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an expanded explanation of syllogisms composed of mutually opposite premises (مقدمات متقابلة). The author classifies verbal opposites into four pairs: 'every, not every', 'every, not one', 'some, not one', and 'some, not some'. In reality, there are only three because 'some, not some' does not constitute genuine opposition. The key is that the two premises must share terms; otherwise, the syllogism fails to produce a self-negation. The author provides two methods for constructing such syllogisms: either by substituting a term with an opposite judgment, or by substituting a universal/particular and reversing the judgment. Examples are given in the first figure, such as 'Every human is evil' and 'Nothing evil is Adamic' yielding 'No human is Adamic'. The conditions for each figure are then detailed: in the first figure, the terms must be synonymous; in the second, the minor and major must be synonymous; in the third, the subject is treated as two things. The author also discusses contraries vs. contradictories in the second figure, and notes that in the third figure, if the major is more specific than the minor, the conclusion is not false (as in the geometry example). The passage ends with a discussion of the third figure, which is incomplete in the OCR.

Two Methods for Composing Syllogisms from Opposite Premises
Methods and first figure examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two methods of substitution to construct a first-figure syllogism from opposite premises.

Core Idea
Substitution of a term or its universal/particular with reversed judgment is the key to hidden syllogisms from opposites.

The author presents two methods for constructing syllogisms from opposite premises: either by substituting the term with an opposite judgment or by substituting a universal/particular and reversing the judgment. These methods are illustrated with first-figure examples. Understanding them allows one to detect deceptive reasoning in jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
Method 1: Replace the term with a word that implies it and judge it with the opposite judgment. Method 2: Replace the term with a universal or particular and judge it with the removal (negative) of the original judgment.
2
Analyze the first example (Method 1)
Consider: 'Every human is evil' and 'Nothing evil is Adamic'. Here 'evil' in the major premise replaces 'human' in the minor. The judgment changes from positive to negative. The conclusion 'No human is Adamic' follows.
Note the synonymy: evil and human are not synonymous, but the trick is that the middle term 'evil' is used to connect.
3
Analyze the second example (Method 2)
Consider: 'Every human is animal' and 'Nothing that actually laughs is animal'. Here 'actually laughs' (a universal) substitutes for 'human'. The judgment is reversed: human is animal affirmatively, but the major denies animal of laugher. Conclusion: 'No human is actual laugher'. Also, it removes animality from human.
The author notes that this also negates animality from human because laugher includes animality.
4
Generalize the condition for first figure
The terms must be synonymous (ترادف) for the syllogism to work. The middle term is repeated, and the major premise is opposite to the minor.
Example: 'Every A is B, no B is C' yields 'No A is C'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

القياس في الجدل على سبيل المبالغة في الإلزام، والمتقابلات في اللفظ أربعة : كل ، ولا كل» ، «كل ، ولا واحد»، «بعض، ولا واحد»، «بعض، ولا بعض». وفي الحقيقية ثلاثة لأن البعض ، ولا بعض من جملة ما لا تقابل فيه. وأما اشتراك المقدمتين في الحدود فذلك من اللوازم وإلا لا يلزم من تركيب القياس منهما سلب الشيء عن نفسه. وإنما يخفى عليه ذلك بأن يعبر اللفظ في المقدمة ويورد بدل اسم الحد ما يراد فيه ويحكم عليه بحكم تقابل ما حكم به على الحد في المقدمة الأولى كما في قولنا كل إنسان شر ولا شيء من الشر بآدمي لينتج لا شيء من الإنسان بآدمي، أو يورد بدل الحد كلية أو جزئية ويحكم عليه برفع الحكم عن الحد: أما الأول فكقولنا كل إنسان ناطق ولا شيء من الحيوان بناطق؛ فإن الحكم على الحيوان حكم على الناطق لما أن الناطق بحسب الحيوان، والحكم على الكلي حكم على الجزئي الذي تحته؛ وحينئذ يلزم سلب الناطق عن الإنسان. وأما الثاني فكقولنا كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الضاحك بالفعل بحيوان فينتج لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل ؛ ويلزم منه أيضًا رفع الحيوانية عن الإنسان لما أن الضاحك بالفعل مشتمل على الحيوانية. ثم إنه إنما يتألف في الشكل الأول أن تكون الحدود مترادفة حتى إذا كرر الوسط بلفظ واحد كانت الكبرى مقابلة للصغرى فجعل المحمول في الصغرى كشيئين؛ وقد وضع أحدهما للآخر إيجابًا في الصغرى وسلبًا في الكبرى كما في المثال الذي مرَّ ذكره. وأما في الشكل الثاني فالترادف بين الأصغر والأكبر لازم لأنه جعل فيه الشيء الواحد كشيئين وحمل عليهما محمول واحد. وكذلك في الشكل الثالث لأنه جعل الشيء الواحد كشيئين وحمل كل واحد منهما على موضوع واحد مثال الشكل الثاني قد مرَّ من قبل، ومثال الشكل الثالث قولنا كل طب علم ولا طب بعلم فليس كل علم بعلم. ثم إنه إذا كان مؤلفًا من المتضادتين في الشكل الثاني يصح أن تكون كل واحدة منهما الصغرى والكبرى. فأما إذا كان مؤلفًا من المتناقضتين فلا يصح إلا وأن تكون الكبرى نفي الكلية منهما موجبة كانت أو سالبة، ويصح أن يكون أحد الطرفين فيه أخص من الآخر كما إذا قلت إن كل علم فاضل ولا شيء من العلوم بفاضل فيكون هذا قياسا من مقدمتين متقابلتين. ثمَّ إنك إذا وضعت بدل العلم الموضوع في أحد الطرفين الطب كان القياس أيضًا من متقابلتين مع أن أحد طرفين أخص من الآخر. وأما في الشكل الثالث فلا يمكن أن يكون القياس فيه مؤلفًا من المتقابلتين ويكون الطرف الأكبر أخص من الأصغر وينتج غير الحق. فإنا إذا قلنا كل هندسة علم ولا شيء من الهندسة بطب ينتج ليس كل علم بطب؛ وهو حق.
Section: On that the syllogism may be composed of mutually opposite premises. The syllogism may be composed of mutually opposite premises; that is, by composing from an affirmative and a negative that are opposites, such as contraries and others, as a stratagem and deception against the interlocutor. And this syllogism is often used in dialectic for the purpose of exaggeration in obligation. The verbal opposites are four: 'every, not every', 'every, not one', 'some, not one', 'some, not some'. And in reality they are three, because 'some, not some' is among what has no opposition. As for the two premises sharing the terms, that is among the necessary conditions; otherwise, composing a syllogism from them does not necessitate the negation of a thing about itself. And it is hidden from him (the opponent) only by substituting in the premise a word for the name of the term that is meant and judging it with the judgment of the opposite of what was judged about the term in the first premise, as in our saying: 'Every human is evil' and 'Nothing evil is Adamic' to yield 'No human is Adamic'. Or he substitutes for the term a universal or particular and judges it with the lifting of the judgment about the term: as for the first, it is like our saying: 'Every human is rational' and 'No animal is rational', for the judgment about animal is a judgment about rational because rational pertains to animal, and the judgment about the universal is a judgment about the particular under it; then the negation of rational about human follows. As for the second, it is like our saying: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Nothing that actually laughs is an animal' to yield 'No human is an actual laugher'; and from it also follows the removal of animality from human because the actual laugher includes animality. Then it is composed in the first figure only when the terms are synonymous, such that when the middle is repeated with one expression, the major premise is opposite to the minor, making the predicate in the minor as two things; and one of them is placed for the other affirmatively in the minor and negatively in the major, as in the example that passed. As for the second figure, synonymy between the minor and major terms is necessary because it makes one thing into two things and predicates a single predicate on them. Similarly in the third figure because it makes one thing into two things and predicates each of them on a single subject. An example of the second figure has passed earlier, and an example of the third figure is our saying: 'Every medicine is knowledge' and 'No medicine is knowledge' so 'Not every knowledge is knowledge'. Then if it is composed from contraries in the second figure, it is valid for either of them to be the minor or the major. But if it is composed from contradictories, it is not valid unless the major premise is the negation of the universal of them, whether affirmative or negative, and it is valid that one of the two terms is more specific than the other, as when you say: 'Every knowledge is virtuous' and 'Nothing of the sciences is virtuous' — this would be a syllogism from two opposite premises. Then if you substitute for 'science' the subject in one of the two terms with 'medicine', the syllogism is also from two opposites, with one term more specific than the other. As for the third figure, it is not possible for the syllogism therein to be composed from two opposites and for the major term to be more specific than the minor and produce a false conclusion. For if we say: 'Every geometry is knowledge' and 'Nothing of geometry is medicine' it yields 'Not every knowledge is medicine', and it is true.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an expanded explanation of syllogisms composed of mutually opposite premises (مقدمات متقابلة). The author classifies verbal opposites into four pairs: 'every, not every', 'every, not one', 'some, not one', and 'some, not some'. In reality, there are only three because 'some, not some' does not constitute genuine opposition. The key is that the two premises must share terms; otherwise, the syllogism fails to produce a self-negation. The author provides two methods for constructing such syllogisms: either by substituting a term with an opposite judgment, or by substituting a universal/particular and reversing the judgment. Examples are given in the first figure, such as 'Every human is evil' and 'Nothing evil is Adamic' yielding 'No human is Adamic'. The conditions for each figure are then detailed: in the first figure, the terms must be synonymous; in the second, the minor and major must be synonymous; in the third, the subject is treated as two things. The author also discusses contraries vs. contradictories in the second figure, and notes that in the third figure, if the major is more specific than the minor, the conclusion is not false (as in the geometry example). The passage ends with a discussion of the third figure, which is incomplete in the OCR.

Conditions for Opposite Premises in Second and Third Figures
Conditions for second and third figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a syllogism from opposite premises in the second or third figure yields a valid or true conclusion.

Core Idea
In the second figure, synonymy between minor and major is required; in the third figure, if the major is more specific than the minor, the conclusion is true.

The author extends the discussion to the second and third figures, explaining the conditions for composing syllogisms from opposite premises. He also distinguishes between contraries and contradictories in the second figure and notes a special case in the third figure where the conclusion is true even if the major is more specific.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition for the second figure
The author states that in the second figure, synonymy between the minor and major terms is necessary. This is because one thing is treated as two and a single predicate is applied to both.
Example from earlier: 'Every human is rational, no animal is rational' yields 'No human is rational'. Here 'human' and 'animal' are not synonymous but the predicate 'rational' is the same.
2
Distinguish contraries from contradictories in second figure
If the premises are contraries (متضادتان), either premise can be the major or minor. If they are contradictories (متناقضتان), the major premise must be the negation of the universal of them.
Contraries: 'Every A is B' and 'No A is B' (both false possible). Contradictories: 'Every A is B' and 'Not every A is B'.
3
Analyze the example for second figure
The author gives: 'Every knowledge is virtuous' and 'Nothing of the sciences is virtuous' – this is a syllogism from opposites with one term more specific (sciences vs knowledge). The conclusion would be something like 'No knowledge is virtuous'? Actually he says it produces a syllogism from opposite premises.
4
Understand the third figure condition
In the third figure, if the major term is more specific than the minor, the conclusion is not false; it is true. Example: 'Every geometry is knowledge' and 'Nothing of geometry is medicine' yields 'Not every knowledge is medicine', which is true.
The author implies that such a syllogism does not produce a falsehood, so it's acceptable even if the major is more specific.

Conditions for Syllogisms of Opposites in the Four Figures
Conditions for syllogisms of opposites in the four figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of a syllogism composed of opposite premises and determine whether the required condition (synonymy or other) is satisfied.

Core Idea
A syllogism from opposite premises is only valid under strict term relations that vary by figure.

In this lesson, we explore the special conditions under which two opposite propositions can serve as premises in a categorical syllogism. The author distinguishes between contraries and contradictories and sets forth specific requirements for each figure. By mastering these conditions, you will be able to assess the validity of such arguments in Islamic dialectical contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure of the syllogism
Determine whether the middle term is subject or predicate in each premise. This classifies the syllogism into first, second, third, or fourth figure.
In the first figure, the middle term is subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor. In the second, it is predicate in both. In the third, subject in both. In the fourth, predicate in the major and subject in the minor.
2
Determine the type of opposition
Are the premises contraries (e.g., 'Every S is P' and 'No S is P') or contradictories (e.g., 'Every S is P' and 'Some S is not P')? The author treats both as 'mutaqabilatan' but distinguishes them in the conditions.
Contraries cannot both be true; contradictories cannot both be true nor both false.
3
Check the specific condition for that figure
For the first and fourth figures, all three terms must be synonymous. For the second figure, the minor and major terms must be synonymous; if the premises are contradictories, the major premise must be the universal negation. For the third figure, composition from opposites is impossible if the major term is more specific than the minor.
Synonymy (taraduf) means the terms have the same referent, ensuring the middle term acts as a common link.
4
Assess validity
If the condition holds, the syllogism yields a necessary conclusion; otherwise, it is invalid or produces a falsehood. Test with examples from the passage.
The author gives examples: the second figure syllogism 'Every human is an animal; no actually laughing is an animal; therefore no human is actually laughing' is valid because synonymy holds. The third figure example 'Every medicine is a science; no medicine is a science; therefore not every science is a science' is invalid because the condition is violated.

الناطق بحسب الحيوان، والحكم على الكلي حكم على الجزئي الذي تحته؛ وحينئذ يلزم سلب الناطق عن الإنسان. وأما الثاني فكقولنا كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الضاحك بالفعل بحيوان فينتج لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل؛ ويلزم منه أيضًا رفع الحيوانية عن الإنسان لما أن الضاحك بالفعل مشتمل على الحيوانية. ثم إنه إنما يتألف في الشكل الأول أن تكون الحدود مترادفة حتى إذا كرر الوسط بلفظ واحد كانت الكبرى مقابلة للصغرى فجعل المحمول في الصغرى كشيئين؛ وقد وضع أحدهما للآخر إيجابًا في الصغرى وسلبًا في الكبرى كما في المثال الذي مرَّ ذكره. وأما في الشكل الثاني فالترادف بين الأصغر والأكبر لازم لأنه جعل فيه الشيء الواحد كشيئين وحمل عليهما محمول واحد. وكذلك في الشكل الثالث لأنه جعل الشيء الواحد كشيئين وحمل كل واحد منهما على موضوع واحد مثال الشكل الثاني قد مرَّ من قبل، ومثال الشكل الثالث قولنا كل طب علم ولا طب بعلم فليس كل علم بعلم. ثم إنه إذا كان مؤلفًا من المتضادتين في الشكل الثاني يصح أن تكون كل واحدة منهما الصغرى والكبرى. فأما إذا كان مؤلفًا من المتناقضتين فلا يصح إلا وأن تكون الكبرى نفي الكلية منهما موجبة كانت أو سالبة، ويصح أن يكون أحد الطرفين فيه أخص من الآخر كما إذا قلت إن كل علم فاضل ولا شيء من العلوم بفاضل فيكون هذا قياسا من مقدمتين متقابلتين. ثمَّ إنك إذا وضعت بدل العلم الموضوع في أحد الطرفين الطب كان القياس أيضًا من متقابلتين مع أن أحد طرفين أخص من الآخر. وأما في الشكل الثالث فلا يمكن أن يكون القياس فيه مؤلفًا من المتقابلتين ويكون الطرف الأكبر أخص من الأصغر وينتج غير الحق. فإنا إذا قلنا كل هندسة علم ولا شيء من الهندسة بطب ينتج ليس كل علم بطب؛ وهو حق. وأما في الشكل الرابع فإنه إنما يكون من المتقابلتين إذا كانت الحدود الثلاثة فيه مترادفة كما في الشكل الأول حتى إذا حكم على الأوسط بحكم ثمَّ حكم به على الأكبر بحكم مخالف للحكم الأول كان مضادا له كما في قولنا كل إنسان بشر ولا شيء من الآدمي بإنسان فبعض البشر ليس بآدمي.
The speaking one in relation to animal, and the judgment about the universal is a judgment about the particular under it; and then the negation of the speaking one from man follows. As for the second, it is like our saying: Every human is an animal, and nothing of the actually laughing is an animal, so it results that nothing of humans is actually laughing; and from it also follows the removal of animality from man because the actually laughing includes animality. Then, in the first figure, it is composed only if the terms are synonymous, so that when the middle is repeated with one word, the major is opposite to the minor, making the predicate in the minor like two things; and one of them is posited for the other affirmatively in the minor and negatively in the major, as in the example that was mentioned. As for the second figure, synonymy between the minor and major is necessary because in it one thing is made like two things and one predicate is predicated of them. Similarly in the third figure because in it one thing is made like two things and each one of them is predicated of one subject. An example of the second figure has passed before, and an example of the third figure is our saying: Every medicine is a science, and no medicine is a science, so not every science is a science. Then, if it is composed of the two contraries in the second figure, it is valid for each one of them to be the minor and the major. But if it is composed of the two contradictories, it is not valid except that the major is the universal negation of them, whether affirmative or negative, and it is valid for one of the two extremes to be more specific than the other, as when you say: Every science is excellent, and nothing of the sciences is excellent, so this is a syllogism from two opposite premises. Then if you substitute the subject science in one of the extremes with medicine, the syllogism is also from two opposites while one extreme is more specific than the other. As for the third figure, it is not possible for the syllogism to be composed of the two opposites and for the major term to be more specific than the minor and produce falsehood. For if we say: Every geometry is a science, and nothing of geometry is medicine, it produces: Not every science is medicine; and it is true. As for the fourth figure, it is only composed of the two opposites if the three terms in it are synonymous, as in the first figure, so that when a judgment is made about the middle then a judgment is made about the major with a judgment contrary to the first judgment, it is opposite to it, as in our saying: Every human is a human, and nothing of the human is a human, so some humans are not human.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory expansion on the conditions for composing syllogisms from opposite propositions (al-mutaqabilat) across the four figures. The author begins by referencing a previous example about 'speaking' (al-natiq) and 'animal' to illustrate how a universal judgment applies to particulars, leading to the negation of 'speaking' from man. Then a second example is given: 'Every human is an animal; nothing actually laughing is an animal' yields 'No human is actually laughing,' and it also implies the removal of animality from man because the laughing thing contains animality. This sets up the discussion of opposites.

The author then states the condition for the first figure: the three terms must be synonymous (mutaradifa), so that when the middle term is repeated with one wording, the major premise becomes opposite to the minor. This means the minor's predicate is treated as two things, one affirmed and the other denied. In the second figure, synonymy between the minor and major terms is necessary because one thing (the minor) is treated as two things and a single predicate is predicated of both. In the third figure, synonymy is also required because one predicate is treated as two and each is predicated of the same subject. Examples: for the second figure, a previous example is referenced; for the third, 'Every medicine is a science; no medicine is a science; therefore, not every science is a science' — an invalid syllogism showing composition from opposites.

Next, the author distinguishes between contraries (al-mutadaddatan) and contradictories (al-mutanaqidatan). In the second figure, if the syllogism is composed of contraries, either premise can serve as the minor or major. If composed of contradictories, the major must be the universal negation of the pair, whether the original was affirmative or negative. Additionally, one extreme can be more specific than the other, as in 'Every science is excellent; nothing of the sciences is excellent' — a syllogism from opposite premises. Substituting 'science' with 'medicine' still yields opposite premises with one extreme more specific.

In the third figure, composition from opposites is impossible if the major term is more specific than the minor and would produce a false conclusion. For example, 'Every geometry is a science; nothing of geometry is medicine' produces 'Not every science is medicine,' which is actually true, not false. Thus the condition fails.

Finally, for the fourth figure, composition from opposites requires the three terms to be synonymous, like in the first figure. When a judgment is made about the middle and then a contrary judgment about the major, the two judgments are opposite. Example: 'Every human is a human; nothing of the human is a human; therefore, some humans are not human.' This demonstrates the necessary synonymy.

الترادف
الشكل الأول
المتضادتان
المتناقضتان

Key Takeaway

?

The Syllogism

When Syllogisms Hide Opposite Premises
Author warns about logicians unknowingly using opposite premises and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect the hidden use of contradictory premises in syllogisms and identify the absurd conclusions they produce.

Core Idea
Opposite premises creep in through corrupt syllogisms, but their absurd conclusions expose the contradiction.

Scholars of logic must be vigilant: it is possible to unknowingly employ a syllogism with opposite premises. This occurs when invalid syllogisms yield a premise that contradicts a true premise you already hold. The result is absurdity. In this lesson, we will dissect this subtle fallacy using the author's example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the true premise
Locate the premise (al-muqaddima al-sadiqa) that is known to be true and serves as a standard. In the example, it is 'Some numbers are odd'.
2
Trace the corrupt syllogisms
Recognize that the person holds invalid syllogisms that produce a false conclusion, such as 'Every number is divisible into two equals'.
3
Derive the contradictory premise
Combine the false conclusion with another true premise (e.g., 'Nothing divisible into two equals is odd') to derive a proposition that contradicts the original true premise, e.g., 'No number is odd'.
4
Form the syllogism from opposites
Now you have two contradictory premises: 'Some numbers are odd' and 'No number is odd'. Use them in a syllogism to see the absurd conclusions (e.g., 'Some numbers are not numbers').
5
Generalize the warning
Understand that such contradictions often arise when the false premise is opposite in type to the true premise's genus, and the reasoner is unaware.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأنه يمكن أن يعرض لأصحاب النظر الوقوف في استعمال القياسات المؤلفة من المتقابلات وهم لا يشعرون به؛ وذلك حيث يكون عند إنسان ما قياسات فاسدة منتجة نتائج فاسدة ويكون في تلك القياسات أو تلك النتائج ما يلزم منه مقدمة فاسدة، ويمكن أن يساوي منهما أي إنتاج ضد ما يكون حق. وحينئذ ينتظم عنده من ذلك الحق ومن ضد ذلك القياس من متقابلتين من غير قصد منه إلى ذلك وشعور به مثلا إذا فرضت المقدّمة الصادقة وهي قولنا بعض الأعداد فرد ويكون عنده قياسات فاسدة لزم منها أو من نتائجها الفاسدة إن كل عدد منقسم بمتساويين؛ فإذا اكتسبت من هذ المقدمة بانضمام قولنا لا شيء من المنقسم بمتساويين بفرد قولنا لا شيء من الأعداد بفرد الذي هو نقيض تلك المقدمة الصادقة. وحينئذ يكون النظر واقعا على مقدمتين متناقضين إحديهما قولنا بعض الأعداد فرد، والثانية قولنا ولا شيء من الأعداد بفرد؛ وإنهما ينتجان من الشكل الثاني بعض الأعداد ليس بعدد، ومن الثالث بعض الأفراد ليس بفرد. ويمكن أن يكون وقوعه على القياس المؤلف من متقابلتين؛ وذلك بأن يكون في القياسات الفاسدة عنده أو في نتائجه مقدمة هي مقابلة لجنس المقدمة الصادقة أو لجزئي تحتها. فإذا خفي الحال كانت تلك المقدمة مقابلة للمقدِّمة الصادقة لما أنه يمكن أن يستنتج منها، ومن مقدمة أخرى مقابل المقدّمة الصادقة مثلا إذا فرضنا أن المقدمة الصادقة قولنا كل علم ظن ثمَّ لزم من أصول آخر كانت عنده أن الطب ليس بظن فإن
And know that it may happen to practitioners of speculation that they stop in the use of syllogisms composed of opposites without being aware of it. That is when a person has corrupt syllogisms producing corrupt conclusions, and in those syllogisms or those conclusions there is something from which a corrupt premise follows, and it is possible that from them [the syllogisms] equality is derived, i.e., the production of the opposite of what is true. Then there is organized for him from that truth and from its opposite a syllogism from two opposite premises without his intention and awareness. For example, if the true premise is assumed, which is our saying 'Some numbers are odd', and he has corrupt syllogisms from which it follows, or from their corrupt conclusions, that 'Every number is divisible into two equals.' Then if from this premise and with the addition of our saying 'Nothing divisible into two equals is odd' we acquire our saying 'Nothing of the numbers is odd', which is the contradictory of that true premise. Then the speculation falls upon two contradictory premises: one is our saying 'Some numbers are odd', and the second is our saying 'No number is odd'; and they produce from the second figure 'Some numbers are not numbers', and from the third 'Some odd things are not odd'. And it is possible that his falling upon the syllogism composed of two opposites is due to the fact that in his corrupt syllogisms or their conclusions there is a premise that is opposite to the genus of the true premise or to a particular under it. So if the situation is hidden, that premise is opposite to the true premise because it is possible to deduce from it, and from another premise opposite to the true premise, for example if we assume that the true premise is our saying 'Every science is opinion', then it follows from other principles that he has that 'Medicine is not opinion', then...
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a warning about a subtle fallacy: logicians may unknowingly employ syllogisms composed of opposite premises (mutaqabilat) without realizing it. The author explains that this occurs when a person holds some corrupt (invalid) syllogisms that produce false conclusions. From these, a corrupt premise may be derived. By combining this premise with a true premise, one might derive the opposite of the true premise, resulting in a syllogism with two opposite premises, all without deliberate intention.

An example is given: Suppose the true premise is 'Some numbers are odd.' The person also has corrupt syllogisms that lead to the false conclusion 'Every number is divisible into two equals.' Now, from this false conclusion and the additional true premise 'Nothing divisible into two equals is odd,' one can derive 'No number is odd,' which is the contradictory of the original true premise ('Some numbers are odd'). Thus, the reasoner ends up with two contradictory premises: 'Some numbers are odd' and 'No number is odd.' These two premises, when used together, produce absurd conclusions: from the second figure, 'Some numbers are not numbers,' and from the third figure, 'Some odd things are not odd.' These are clearly false and show the validity of the original true premise.

The author concludes that this situation arises because among the corrupt syllogisms or their conclusions, there is a premise that is opposite to the genus of the true premise or to a particular under it. If this goes unnoticed, that premise is taken as opposite to the true premise, leading to a syllogism from opposites. Another example: if the true premise is 'Every science is opinion,' and from the person's principles it follows that 'Medicine is not opinion,' then a syllogism from opposites occurs, but the text cuts off mid-sentence.

المقدمة الصادقة
المتناقضتان

D) Some numbers are odd and every number is odd.

Key Takeaway
Corrupt syllogisms can produce a premise that contradicts a true premise, leading to a syllogism from opposites and absurd conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Then, using another true premise, you conclude 'It is not the case that each Muslim must pray five times a day.' Identify the contradictory pair and the absurd conclusions that would follow.
?

Conditions for a Valid Reductio ad Absurdum (Khalf)
Critically examines the structure and validity conditions of reductio ad absurdum arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the validity of a reductio ad absurdum (khalf) argument by checking whether the impossibility follows solely from the contradictory of the desired conclusion.

Core Idea
A valid khalf requires that the impossibility (muhal) be entailed by the contradictory (naqid al-matlub) alone, with no help from other premises.

In this lesson, we examine a critical discussion from the logical tradition on when a reductio ad absurdum argument truly proves its point. The author carefully distinguishes a valid khalf from common pitfalls where the absurdity might originate from other premises. By analyzing Ibn Sina's citation and worked examples, you will learn to spot invalid uses of khalf and apply the correct condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradictory and the impossibility
Locate the naqid al-matlub (contradictory of the desired) and the muhal (impossibility) in the argument. The validity hinges on whether the muhal depends on the naqid alone.
2
Apply the removal test
Remove all premises except the contradictory. Check if the impossibility still follows. If it does not, the khalf is invalid because the impossibility may be caused by another premise.
This test is explicitly stated in Ibn Sina's quote: '...if the falsehood follows even if that premise is removed and not used in the syllogism at all.'
3
Recognize two invalid types
First type: the terms of the muhal and its syllogism share nothing with the contradictory. Second type: the terms share but the impossibility actually follows from a separate syllogistic chain, not from the contradictory itself.
The text gives examples: the diagonal/side argument (unrelated) and the variable example with B, A, D, C (related but faulty).
4
Ensure connectivity and singularity
A valid khalf must be a single, connected syllogism where the terms of the impossibility, its syllogism, and the desired conclusion are connected. There must be no extraneous syllogisms or redundant premises.
The author emphasizes that disjointed syllogisms cannot produce the required absurdity in the way a single, cohesive khalf does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذكور ويضم إلى شيء آخر حتى يلزم منه المحال، أو تقاس بعده قياسات حتى لزم منها محال . ثم يقال ويلزم من ذلك أن يكون نقيض المطلوب محالا. ثم للمعترض أن يقول لم قلتم بأن ذلك المحال يلزم من نقيض المطلوب ولا يمتنع أن يكون لزومه من الغير مفردًا كان ذلك الغير أو مركبا؛ وأيما كان فلا يلزم من استحالة ذلك استحالة نقيض المطلوب. وقد مر من قبل أنه لا يلزم من استحالة المجموع استحالة كل جزء من أجزائه. وإن قال المدعي في هذه الصورة هو أن نقيض المطلوب مع الغير وهو الذي يكون صادقا في نفسه أو ممكن أن يكون صادقًا في نفسه، ولما لزم منه ومن ذلك الغير محال. ومن المعلوم أنه لا يمكن أن يلزم من ذلك الغير لاستحالة أن يكون الممكن مستلزما للمحال على الخصوص أن يكون صادقًا فنقول هذا مسلم بأنه لا يلزم من ذلك الغير لكن لم قلتم بأنه يلزم من نقيض المطلوب بل لا يلزم منه أيضًا؛ إذ المستلزم لذلك المحال هو المجموع المركب منه ومن غيره، ولا يلزم منه أن يكون لازما من هذا الجزء أو من ذلك كما مر . ولأنه إذا كان لازما للنقيض كان انضمامه إلى غيره ضايعا في إثبات ذلك المحال؛ والمستلزم للشيء لا يفتقر في استلزامه إلى الغير البتة.
وقد أشار الشيخ في الشفاء إلى هذا حيث يقول: «وإنما يمكن أن يقال ذلك إذا كان الكذب يلزم وإن رفعت تلك المقدمة ولم تستعمل في القياس أصلا (۱). ومن زعم بأن كلام الشيخ في هذا المقام لا يكون كما ينبغي فزعمه باطل لا يكون كما ينبغي. ثمَّ إن هذا الاعتبار لا يكون في المستقيم لأن المستقيم لا يقصد فيه إنتاج كذب من وضع شيء مناقض للمطلوب بل هذا في الخلف إذا كان نقيض الموضوع سواء وضع أو رفع لا يعبر حكم اللازم من الكذب ولا يكون سببًا لإنتاج المحال . فلا يلزم أن يكون محالا؛ وهذا على وجوه لأنه إما أن تكون الحدود التي للمحال والقياسه غير مشاركة للنقيض البتة، وإما أن تكون مشاركة لكن المحال يلزم عن شيء آخر مثال الأول لو أن أحدا أراد أن يبين أن القُطر غير مشارك للضلع فاستعمل فيه قياس بين في أن لا حركة ثمَّ قال وهذا محال فإذن القطر مشارك للضلع ؛ وهذا هو أظهر ما في اللباب. أما مثال ما يؤخذ في المحال وقياسه حدود تشارك وتتصل بحدود النقيض فذلك أن نقول ليس كل ب أ) وإلا فليكن كل ب أ وليكن كل «دج» وكل «ج ب» وكل ب أ) فإذن كل دب»، هذا خلف. فإذن ليس كل ب أ)؛ فهذا قد وضع فيه ما ليس بسبب سببًا لأن قولنا كل دب يكون نتيجة عن مقدمتيه، وإن لم نقل كل «ب أ». وأيضًا من الجانب الآخر بأن نقول كل «ب أ» وكل «أج» وكل ج د » فكل «أد» ؛ وهذا خلف فإن هذا أيضًا وضع ما ليس بسبب سببًا. وذلك لأن قولنا كل ب أ) وإن رفع بقي القياس المنتج الخلف بل يجب أن تكون حدود المحال وحدود قياسه وحدود المطلوب متّصلة. ويكون مع ذلك بحيث إذا رفع النقيض لم يلزم المحال بل يكون المحال إنما لزم لوضعه لا غير فيكون القياس المركب متصل التركيب لا حشو فيه؛ وليس قياسات مختلفة لا وصلة بينها. فإن الكذب لا يمكن أن يجتمع عن قياسات كثيرة لا تتصل اتصالا بضربه كقياس واحد؛ فإنها إذا اجتمعت ولم تتصل فإما أن يكون الكذب لازما عن واحد منها وإن رفع البواقي. وإما أن لا يلزم عنها شيء بالشركة، وإن كذب نتائجها كلها أيضًا لم ينتفع بجميعها في إبطال شيء أو إثباته مثل قياسات مختلفة على أن المتوازية تلتقي، وأن المثلث زواياه أعظم من قائمتين وغير ذلك: فإن جميع أصناف النتائج كاذبة لا تتصل قياساتها لا يلزم منه شيء على الوجه الذي يلزم في قياس الخلف.
...the mentioned [premise] and is joined with something else until an impossibility follows from it, or syllogisms are appended after it until an impossibility follows from them. Then it is said: 'And from that it follows that the contradictory of the desired [conclusion] is impossible.' Then the objector may say: 'Why do you claim that that impossibility follows from the contradictory of the desired, and it is not impossible that its necessitation is from the other, whether that other is simple or composite? And whatever it is, the impossibility of that does not necessitate the impossibility of the contradictory of the desired. And it has passed earlier that the impossibility of the composite does not necessitate the impossibility of every part of its parts.' And if the claimant says in this form that the contradictory of the desired with the other, and it is that which is true in itself or possible to be true in itself, and when an impossibility follows from it and from that other, and it is known that it is impossible that from that other [alone] — due to the impossibility that the possible necessitates an impossibility — that it be true, then we say: 'This is conceded, that it does not follow from that other, but why do you claim that it follows from the contradictory of the desired? Rather, it does not follow from it either, for the entailing of that impossibility is the composite composed of it and its other, and it does not necessitate that it be a necessary entailment from this part or that, as has passed. And because if it were necessary for the contradictory, its joining to another would be wasted in proving that impossibility; and what necessitates something does not need another in its necessitation at all.'
And the Sheikh [Ibn Sina] indicated this in al-Shifa where he says: 'That can only be said if the falsehood follows even if that premise is removed and not used in the syllogism at all' (1). And whoever claims that the Sheikh's speech in this place is not as it should be, his claim is false and not as it should be. Then this consideration is not in the direct [syllogism] because in the direct [syllogism] one does not intend to produce falsehood by positing something contradictory to the desired; rather this is in the reductio (khalf) when the contradictory of the subject, whether it is posited or removed, does not change the judgment of what follows from the falsehood and does not become a cause for producing impossibility. So it does not necessitate that it be impossible. And this is in several modes: either the terms of the impossibility and its syllogism do not share [anything] with the contradictory at all, or they share but the impossibility follows from something else. Example of the first: if someone wants to show that the diagonal is not commensurable with the side, and uses in it a syllogism proving that there is no motion, then says: 'And this is impossible, so the diagonal is commensurable with the side.' And this is the most apparent [weakness] in al-Lubab. As for the example of what is taken in the impossibility and its syllogism where terms share and connect with the terms of the contradictory: that we say: 'Not every B is A; otherwise, let every B be A, and let every D be C, and every C be B, and every B is A; so every D is B; this is a contradiction. So not every B is A.' This has placed what is not a cause as a cause, because our saying 'every D is B' is a conclusion from its two premises, even if we do not say 'every B is A'. Also from the other side: that we say: 'Every B is A, and every A is C, and every C is D, so every A is D; this is a contradiction.' For this also places what is not a cause as a cause. That is because our saying 'every B is A', even if removed, the syllogism producing the contradiction remains. Rather, the terms of the impossibility and the terms of its syllogism and the terms of the desired must be connected. And with that, it must be that if the contradictory is removed, the impossibility does not follow; rather the impossibility only follows from its positing, not otherwise. So the composite syllogism is of connected composition with no redundancy in it; and it is not different syllogisms with no connection between them. For falsehood cannot be gathered from many syllogisms that do not connect in the way that a single syllogism connects; for if they are gathered and do not connect, then either the falsehood follows from one of them even if the others are removed, or nothing follows from them collectively; and even if all their conclusions are false, no benefit is obtained from all of them in refuting or proving something, like different syllogisms that parallel lines meet, and that the triangle's angles are greater than two right angles, and so on: for all kinds of false conclusions whose syllogisms do not connect, nothing follows from them in the way that follows in a reductio syllogism.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the reductio ad absurdum (khalf) argument, specifying the conditions under which it is valid. It begins by presenting a common but flawed claim: that if an impossibility (muhal) follows from the conjunction of the contradictory of the desired proposition (naqid al-matlub) and some other premise, then the contradictory itself must be impossible. The author, following the expositor's voice, challenges this by noting that the impossibility might instead stem solely from the other premise or from the composite as a whole, not specifically from the contradictory. He references an earlier principle that the impossibility of a composite does not imply the impossibility of each part.

The author then cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, quoting that the falsehood must persist even if the other premise is removed entirely; otherwise, the reductio is invalid. He dismisses objections to Ibn Sina's position. He distinguishes between direct syllogisms (mustaqim), which aim for truth, and reductio arguments, which assume the contradictory to derive an impossibility. Two problematic cases are identified: first, where the terms of the impossibility are completely unrelated to the contradictory, such as using a fallacious geometrical proof that relies on an irrelevant premise about motion; second, where the terms are related but the impossibility actually follows from a separate syllogistic chain, not from the contradictory itself. He gives logical examples with variables B, A, D, C to illustrate how a conclusion can be derived from premises independent of the contradictory, making the reductio faulty.

Finally, the author stipulates the correct condition: the terms of the impossibility, its syllogism, and the desired conclusion must be connected, and upon removing the contradictory, the impossibility must no longer follow. The reductio must be a single, cohesive syllogism without extraneous elements. He compares this to disjointed syllogisms with false conclusions that cannot collectively produce the intended absurdity, using geometric examples like parallel lines meeting or triangle angle sum being greater than 180 degrees.

Throughout, the commentary follows the author's logical progression: initial claim, objection, citation of authority, classification of errors, and final resolution of the valid form.

Conditions for a Valid Reductio ad Absurdum (Khalf)
Author explains when a composition producing impossibility is valid in reductio proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a reductio ad absurdum argument satisfies the condition of proper term-connection.

Core Idea
A valid khalf requires that the impossibility follows only from the opposite, not from independent premises.

In this passage from the Lubab, the author clarifies a critical condition for proof by contradiction (khalf). Not every argument that reaches an impossibility is a valid reductio; the terms of the syllogism leading to impossibility must be connected to the opposite proposition being denied. You will learn to distinguish a proper khalf from an invalid one by examining how the impossibility relates to the assumption of the opposite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of invalid khalf
The author describes two scenarios where a reductio fails: (a) the terms of the impossibility and its syllogism share nothing with the opposite (e.g., using a syllogism about motion to disprove a geometric claim), and (b) the terms share but the impossibility follows from something other than the opposite (e.g., the examples with logical variables).
These failures occur because the contradiction does not depend on the negation of the target proposition.
2
Extract the condition for a valid khalf
Find the sentence beginning 'بل يجب أن تكون حدود المحال...' This states that the terms of the impossible, its syllogism, and the desired conclusion must be connected, and when the opposite is removed, the impossibility must not follow. The impossibility must arise solely from positing the opposite.
This ensures the reductio is not 'contaminated' by extraneous assumptions.
3
Analyze the variable example
The author gives an example using variables ب, أ, ج, د. Trace the argument: assume the opposite of 'Not every B is A' (i.e., 'Every B is A'), then add premises 'Every D is J' and 'Every J is B'. The conclusion 'Every D is B' follows from the latter two premises alone, independent of 'Every B is A'. Thus the contradiction is not properly tied to the opposite.
The same problem appears in the second variable example: 'Every B is A, every A is J, every J is D, therefore every A is D'—the conclusion does not require 'Every B is A'.
4
Contrast with disconnected syllogisms
The author warns against using multiple unrelated false claims (e.g., 'parallel lines meet' and 'triangle angles sum to more than 180°') to produce a contradiction. Such disconnected syllogisms cannot yield a valid reductio because the falsehood does not stem from a single assumption of the opposite.
A valid khalf must be a single composite syllogism with connected terms, not a collection of separate arguments.

يكون سببًا لإنتاج المحال . فلا يلزم أن يكون محالا؛ وهذا على وجوه لأنه إما أن تكون الحدود التي للمحال والقياسه غير مشاركة للنقيض البتة، وإما أن تكون مشاركة لكن المحال يلزم عن شيء آخر مثال الأول لو أن أحدا أراد أن يبين أن القُطر غير مشارك للضلع فاستعمل فيه قياس بين في أن لا حركة ثمَّ قال وهذا محال فإذن القطر مشارك للضلع ؛ وهذا هو أظهر ما في اللباب. أما مثال ما يؤخذ في المحال وقياسه حدود تشارك وتتصل بحدود النقيض فذلك أن نقول ليس كل ب أ) وإلا فليكن كل ب أ وليكن كل «دج» وكل «ج ب» وكل ب أ) فإذن كل دب»، هذا خلف. فإذن ليس كل ب أ)؛ فهذا قد وضع فيه ما ليس بسبب سببًا لأن قولنا كل دب يكون نتيجة عن مقدمتيه، وإن لم نقل كل «ب أ». وأيضًا من الجانب الآخر بأن نقول كل «ب أ» وكل «أج» وكل ج د » فكل «أد» ؛ وهذا خلف فإن هذا أيضًا وضع ما ليس بسبب سببًا. وذلك لأن قولنا كل ب أ) وإن رفع بقي القياس المنتج الخلف بل يجب أن تكون حدود المحال وحدود قياسه وحدود المطلوب متّصلة. ويكون مع ذلك بحيث إذا رفع النقيض لم يلزم المحال بل يكون المحال إنما لزم لوضعه لا غير فيكون القياس المركب متصل التركيب لا حشو فيه؛ وليس قياسات مختلفة لا وصلة بينها. فإن الكذب لا يمكن أن يجتمع عن قياسات كثيرة لا تتصل اتصالا بضربه كقياس واحد؛ فإنها إذا اجتمعت ولم تتصل فإما أن يكون الكذب لازما عن واحد منها وإن رفع البواقي. وإما أن لا يلزم عنها شيء بالشركة، وإن كذب نتائجها كلها أيضًا لم ينتفع بجميعها في إبطال شيء أو إثباته مثل قياسات مختلفة على أن المتوازية تلتقي، وأن المثلث زواياه أعظم من قائمتين وغير ذلك: فإن جميع أصناف النتائج كاذبة لا تتصل قياساتها لا يلزم منه شيء على الوجه الذي يلزم في قياس الخلف.
It is a cause for producing impossibility. But it is not necessary that it be impossible; and this is in ways: either the terms of the impossible and its syllogism do not share with the opposite at all, or they share but the impossibility follows from something else. Example of the first: if someone wanted to show that the diameter does not share with the side, and he used in that a syllogism proving that there is no motion, then said: this is impossible, therefore the diameter shares with the side; this is the clearest in the Lubab. As for an example of what is taken in the impossible and its syllogism, terms that share and connect with the terms of the opposite: that we say: 'Not every B is A'? Otherwise, let every B be A, and let every D be J, and every J be B, and every B be A; then every D is B. This is a contradiction. So not every B is A. For in this, what is not a cause has been placed as a cause because our statement 'every D is B' is a result from its two premises, even if we do not say 'every B is A.' Also from the other side: that we say: 'Every B is A, and every A is J, and every J is D, then every A is D.' This is a contradiction, for this also places what is not a cause as a cause. That is because our statement 'every B is A' even if removed, the syllogism producing the contradiction remains. Rather, it is necessary that the terms of the impossible, the terms of its syllogism, and the terms of the desired conclusion be connected. And with that, it should be such that when the opposite is removed, the impossibility does not follow; rather, the impossibility only follows from its positing, not otherwise. So the composite syllogism has a connected composition with no redundancy; it is not different syllogisms with no connection between them. For falsehood cannot be gathered from many syllogisms that are not connected in the manner of one syllogism. Because if they come together without connection, then either the falsehood follows from one of them, even if the rest are removed, or nothing follows from them jointly; and even if all their conclusions are false, one does not benefit from all of them in refuting or proving something, like different syllogisms that parallel lines meet, and that the triangle's angles are greater than two right angles, etc. For all kinds of false conclusions, if their syllogisms are not connected, nothing follows from them in the way that follows in a reductio syllogism.

Commentary
This passage is about the conditions for a valid proof by contradiction (khalf). The author explains that when a syllogism leads to an impossibility (muhal), it does not automatically refute the opposite; the terms must be properly connected. He distinguishes two scenarios: first, where the terms of the impossible and its syllogism share nothing with the opposite, giving an example from geometry (showing that a diameter is not a side using a syllogism about motion, then concluding the opposite by contradiction). Second, where the terms do share and connect, illustrated with logical variables (ب, أ, ج, د) demonstrating a proper reductio. The author emphasizes that in a valid khalf, if we remove the opposite (the negation of the desired conclusion), the impossibility should not follow; instead, the impossibility follows only from the positing of the opposite. The syllogism must be a single connected composition without redundancy, not multiple disconnected syllogisms. He warns that disconnected false conclusions, like false claims about parallel lines or triangle angles, do not produce a valid reductio.

Simultaneous Knowledge and Ignorance: Syllogistic Analysis
Author examines how one can know and be ignorant of the same thing through syllogistic reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two ways in which a person can know and be ignorant of the same thing, and explain each using categorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
Knowledge and ignorance can coexist when premises are not arranged in the productive order.

This passage tackles an epistemological puzzle: how can someone know and not know something at the same time? The author employs categorical syllogisms to distinguish between potential and actual knowledge, drawing an analogy with dialectical refutation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for recognizing how we can possess the logical components of a proof without reaching its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dialectical analogy
The author compares a person's inner reasoning to a responder in debate who concedes premises that lead to his refutation without realizing it because they are not productively ordered.
2
Distinguish potential and actual knowledge
Notice that a person may have the premises (potential knowledge) but not the ordered conclusion (actual knowledge).
3
Analyze the first case
Two people can hold opposite beliefs from the same set of premises, but a single person cannot hold both opposite conclusions actually. Formalize the syllogisms: e.g., every D is B, every J is B, every B is A, every J is A. One believes every B is A (true), another believes no J is A (false).
4
Analyze the second case
One person can actually know some conclusions (e.g., every D is A and every B is A) while remaining ignorant of another (every J is A) because he does not combine the premises every J is B and every B is A. He has potential knowledge but not actual.
5
Synthesize the explanation
The coexistence of knowledge and ignorance is due to the failure to actualize potential knowledge from given premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في أنه كيف يمكن أن يعلم الشيء ويجهل معا وإنه كيف يمكن أن يعلم ويظن به مقابل ما يعلم أنه كما أن المجيب قد تسلم مقدمات يلزم منها تبكيته ولا يشعر لأن تسليمها منه لا على الترتيب المنتج كذلك الإنسان فيما بينه وبين نفسه قد تكون عنده المقدمات التي يجب أن تعلم مع العلم بها شيء آخر. فيجهل ذلك الشيء لما أن المقدمات لا تكون حاضرة في ذهنه مرتبة في علمه بالفعل. وكذلك كيف يمكن أن يحهل الشيء ويعلم معا وأن يعلم ويظن به مقابل ما يعلم: فنقول إن السبب في أن يكون بالشيء علم وظن مقابلين بقياسين، أو أحدهما بقياس والآخر ليس بقياس؛ وذلك على جهتين إحديهما تستحيل أن يكون في إنسان واحد في وقت واحد بل قد يقع لإنسانين. وذلك إنه إذا كان مثلا كل د ب و ج ب لا واسطة ثمَّ كل ب أ وكل ج أ أيضا: فإن اعتقد واحدٌ أن ب أ وهو حق، واعتقد الآخر أن لا شيء من ج أ وهو باطل؛ ثم قرن أن كل د ب وأن كل د ج من ذلك اعتقدا عقدين متقابلين. وأما الواحد فلا يمكن أن تنعقد عنده القياسات معا بالفعل فيرى شيئًا وضده من جهة إنتاجها إياهما معا. والجهة الثانية هي التي يمكن في واحد؛ وذلك أن ينعقد أن لا شيء من ج أ؛ ومع ذلك ينعقد في نفسه مقدمات قياس على هذه الصفة أن كل د ج وكل ج ب وكل ب أ، فإنه ربما التفت فعلم من هذا بالفعل أن كل د أ وعلم أن كل ب أ ولم يعلم أن كل ج أ بالفعل. وكان يجب أن تعلم ذلك إذا صرف إليه تأمله؛ إذ العلم بأن كل ج ب وكل ب أ يكون علما بالقوة أن كل ج أ قوة يصير بالفعل إذا أخطر مما على الترتيب قاصدا به أن يعلم لكنه لم يفعل ويظن أن لا شيء من ج أ، فالذي يعلمه ليس يعلمه إلا من جهة العلم بالكلي الذي يلزم منه أن يعلم وهو أن كل ب أ.
Chapter: On how it is possible for a thing to be known and unknown simultaneously, and how it is possible for one to know and yet hold a conjecture opposite to what he knows. For just as the responder may concede premises that entail his being silenced without realizing it, because his conceding them is not in the productive order, similarly a person in his inner self may have the premises that should lead to knowledge of something else along with knowledge of that thing, yet he remains ignorant of that other thing because the premises are not present in his mind arranged in actual knowledge. And likewise how can a thing be both unknown and known simultaneously, and how can one know and yet hold a conjecture opposite to what he knows? We say: The reason for having both knowledge and a contrary conjecture about a thing is due to two syllogisms, or one being a syllogism and the other not. This occurs in two ways: one of them is impossible in a single person at a single time, but can happen to two persons. That is, if for example every D is B and every J is B without a middle, then every B is A and every J is A also. If one believes that every B is A (which is true), and another believes that no J is A (which is false), then they combine that every D is B and every D is J, so they hold two opposite beliefs. As for a single person, it is impossible for the syllogisms to be actualized simultaneously in his mind so that he sees both a thing and its opposite from their production together. The second way is possible in one person: that he believes that no J is A, yet at the same time there are present in his mind premises of a syllogism of this form: every D is J, every J is B, every B is A. Then he might focus and actually know from this that every D is A, and he knows that every B is A, but he does not actually know that every J is A. And he should know that if he turned his attention to it. For knowledge that every J is B and every B is A is potential knowledge that every J is A, a potentiality that becomes actual if he brings it to mind in order intending to know, but he does not do so, and he conjectures that no J is A. So what he knows, he only knows by way of the universal knowledge from which it follows that he knows, namely that every B is A.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage addresses an epistemological puzzle: how can someone simultaneously know and be ignorant of the same thing, or know something yet hold a contrary opinion? The author draws an analogy with the dialectical situation: a responder may concede premises that entail his own refutation without realizing it because the premises are not arranged productively. Similarly, a person may possess premises that logically imply a certain conclusion, yet fail to actualize that knowledge because the premises are not mentally ordered. The author then distinguishes two cases. The first case is impossible for a single person at one time but possible between two people: for example, if 'every D is B', 'every J is B', 'every B is A', and 'every J is A' are true, one person may believe 'every B is A' (true) and another 'no J is A' (false). Combining the premises yields opposite beliefs, but a single person cannot actualize both syllogisms simultaneously to see both a thing and its opposite. The second case is possible in one person: someone may believe 'no J is A' while also having in mind the premises 'every D is J', 'every J is B', 'every B is A'. From these he may actually know 'every D is A' and 'every B is A', but not 'every J is A'. He has the potential knowledge of 'every J is A' because the premises 'every J is B' and 'every B is A' are there, but he fails to combine them in the productive order. As a result, he remains ignorant of that conclusion and even holds a contrary conjecture. His knowledge of 'every B is A' is universal, but he does not apply it to the particular case of J. This explains how knowledge and ignorance can coexist: the premises are known but not synthesized into the specific conclusion.

العلم بالقوة
العلم بالفعل
الظن
التبكيت

Knowledge and ignorance can coexist when the premises are known but not synthesized into the specific conclusion due to lack of productive arrangement.

Converting Syllogistic Conclusions via Premise Conversions
Author explains how to derive the converse of a conclusion by converting premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the converse of a first-figure categorical syllogism's conclusion by converting and reordering its premises.

Core Idea
Converting a conclusion requires converting both premises and forming new syllogisms.

In this lesson, we will explore how a syllogism's conclusion can be 'swapped' (subject and predicate exchanged) by converting the premises and constructing new syllogisms. The author demonstrates this using a first-figure example with terms أ, ب, ج. This skill is crucial for understanding how conclusions can be transformed in demonstration, a key tool in Islamic kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Locate the original syllogism in the text: minor premise 'Every ج is ب', major premise 'Every ب is أ', conclusion 'Every ج is أ'. Note the order: minor (subject ج), major (predicate أ), middle ب.
In first figure, the middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor.
2
State the desired converse conclusion
The author wants to derive 'Every أ is ج' as the converse of the original conclusion. This is the proposition with subject and predicate swapped.
Note: This is not simple immediate conversion of a universal affirmative (which yields a particular), but a derived result through premise manipulations.
3
Convert the minor premise
Take the converse conclusion 'Every أ is ج' and the original major premise 'Every ب is أ'. Combine them in the first figure: 'Every ب is أ' and 'Every أ is ج' yields 'Every ب is ج'. This new conclusion is the converse of the original minor premise.
The original minor was 'Every ج is ب'; its converse is 'Every ب is ج'.
4
Convert the major premise
Take the converse conclusion 'Every أ is ج' and the original minor premise 'Every ج is ب'. Combine: 'Every أ is ج' and 'Every ج is ب' yields 'Every أ is ب'. This is the converse of the original major premise (which was 'Every ب is أ').
The major premise converts to 'Every أ is ب', moving to second figure.
5
Verify the process yields the converse conclusion
Recognize that the two new syllogisms (steps 3 and 4) produce the converted premises. The author states: 'just as the premises are converted, so too the conclusion is converted by the conversion of the two premises.' This shows that the converse conclusion can be derived if one has the original premises and performs this manipulation.
This procedure works specifically for the given form; the author also notes adjustments if the major premise is negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في عكس النتائج وهنا اعتبارات تعرض للقياس والمقدّمات بسبب أحوال في الحدود فنقول إذا قلنا كل ج ب » وكل ب أ) ينتج كل (ج) أ»؛ ثم إذا انعكس هذا يصح إن كل «أج» فيلزم أن تنعكس الصغرى. وذلك لأنك تقول كل «ب أ» وكل «أج» فكل ب ج ؛ ويلزم أن تنعكس الكبرى لأنك تقول كل «أج» وكل ج ب» فكل «أب». فإن كانت الكبرى سالبة فعلت كل ج ب ولا شيء من ب أ) فلا شيء من (ج) أ)؛ فالكبرى تنعكس. وكما أنها تنعكس فكذلك النتيجة لانعكاسهما . وذلك لأن الكبرى إذا انعكست صارت إلى الشكل الثاني وصلحت أن تكون صغرى؛ فأنتجت عكس النتيجة. وكذلك إن انعكست الصغرى. والنتيجة السالبة انعكست الكبرى السالبة لأنه إذا كان كل ب ج ) ولا شيء من «أج»؛ وهو عكس النتيجة ينتج لا شيء من «أب».
Chapter on the conversion of conclusions. Here there are considerations that apply to the syllogism and premises due to states of the terms. We say: if we say 'Every ج is ب' and 'Every ب is أ', it produces 'Every ج is أ'. Then if this is converted, it is correct that 'Every أ is ج'; so it is necessary that the minor premise be converted. That is because you say 'Every ب is أ' and 'Every أ is ج', so 'Every ب is ج'; and it is necessary that the major premise be converted because you say 'Every أ is ج' and 'Every ج is ب', so 'Every أ is ب'. If the major premise is negative, you say 'Every ج is ب' and 'No ب is أ', so 'No ج is أ'; thus the major premise is converted. And just as it is converted, so too the conclusion is converted by the conversion of the two premises. That is because when the major premise is converted, it becomes the second figure and is suitable as a minor premise; so it produces the converse of the conclusion. And similarly if the minor premise is converted. The negative conclusion converts the negative major premise, because if it is 'Every ب is ج' and 'No أ is ج' – which is the converse of the conclusion – it produces 'No أ is ب'.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of how the conclusion of a categorical syllogism can be converted by converting the premises. The author begins by stating that there are considerations regarding the syllogism and premises arising from the states of the terms. He then uses an example in the first figure: 'Every ج is ب' and 'Every ب is أ' yields 'Every ج is أ'. If we want to convert this conclusion to 'Every أ is ج', we must convert the premises. First, convert the minor premise: 'Every ب is أ' becomes (by conversion) something that together with the converted conclusion yields a new syllogism. Specifically, taking 'Every ب is أ' and 'Every أ is ج' gives 'Every ب is ج', which is the converse of the original minor. Similarly, to convert the major premise, we take 'Every أ is ج' and 'Every ج is ب' to get 'Every أ is ب'. If the major premise is negative, e.g., 'Every ج is ب' and 'No ب is أ' yields 'No ج is أ'; here the major premise 'Every ج is ب' itself is not negative, but the conclusion is negative. The author then explains that converting the major premise moves it to the second figure, where it can serve as a minor premise to produce the converse of the original conclusion. The same applies if the minor premise is converted. Finally, he notes that a negative conclusion converts the negative major premise: if 'Every ب is ج' and the converse of the conclusion 'No أ is ج' are taken, they yield 'No أ is ب'. This lesson is crucial for understanding how to manipulate categorical syllogisms through conversion, a necessary skill in demonstration.

D) Neither premise needs conversion

Key Takeaway
To obtain the converse of a syllogistic conclusion, both premises must be converted and rearranged into new syllogisms.

Abstract Conversion of Mutuals and Opposites
Abstract definition of conversion of mutuals and opposites with variables.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to formalize the conversion of mutually convertible terms using logical variables and derive the necessary implications.

Core Idea
Mutual conversion of terms implies a chain of implications expressible through compound syllogisms.

In this passage, the author introduces the concept of conversion of mutuals and opposites using abstract variables. He shows how if two pairs of terms are mutually convertible, certain relations follow. This abstract exercise lays the groundwork for applying the same reasoning to concrete Islamic metaphysical concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the variables and their relations
The passage uses A, B, C, D. Each pair (A,B) and (C,D) are convertible. Note also the relationship between the pairs.
2
Trace the conditional chain
Follow the argument: if A is found in a subject, B is found; if C is absent, D is absent; etc. This is a chain of implications.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that when B is present, D is absent, and vice versa. Also, if one of the pair is present, the other is present. This shows the logical relationship.
4
Relate to conversion of opposites
Recognize that the variables are placeholders for real terms; the logical structure applies to any mutually convertible pair and their opposites.
