فيه «ب» لا يوجد فيه «د». وكذلك عندما يوجد فيه «د» يجب أن يوجد فيه «ج» ولا يوجدا؛ فيجب أن لا يوجد فيه «ب» وإلا فقد وجدا وأقول أيهما يوجد فالآخر يوجد لأنه إذا لم يكن فيه «د» ويكون فيه «ب» لأنه إذا لم يكن فيه (د) لم يكن فيه «ج» وإلا كان «ج» ليس (ب) د». فإذا لم يكن فيه (ج) كان فيه «أ» وكل «أب» لكان فيه «ب». وكذلك بالعكس. ومثال أن المكوّن والفاسد إذا انعكس أحدهما على الآخر فغير المكون وغير الفاسد ينعكس أحدهما على الآخر؛ وكل شيء إما مكوّن وإما غير مكوّن فكل شيء إما فاسد وإما غير فاسد وهذان قياسان مركبان أحدهما يبين أن الشيء يكون موصوفًا بأحد هذين، والثاني أنهما لا يجتمعان فيه معا فيتبين منهما المطلوب. فأما الأول منهما فهو إن كل شيء إما مكوّن وإما غير مكوّن وكل مكوّن فاسد، وكل غير مكوّن غير فاسد ينتج كل شيء لا يخلو عن كونه فاسدا أو غير فاسد. ولكن معنى لا يخلو هنا ليس هو أنه لا يجتمعان فيه ولا يزولان عنه بل إنه لا يوجد مفارقًا لهما جميعًا وإن جاز اجتماعهما فيه. وهذا في نفسه قياس مركب من قياسين أحدهما أن كل شيء إما مكون وإما غير مكوّن، وكل مكوّن فاسد ينتج أن كل شيء لا يخلو من أن يكون فاسدًا أو غير مكوّن. ولو قلت كل شيء إما فاسد وإما غير مكوّن، وكل غير مكون غير فاسد ينتج أن كل شيء إما فاسد وإما غير فاسد ولكن ليس على معنى أنه لا يمكن اجتماعهما فيه كما مرَّ بل يفتقر في إتمام ذلك إلى قياس آخر. وهو أيضًا مركب من قياسات وهو أنه لا شيء مما هو مكوّن فغير مكوّن وكل غير فاسد غير مكوّن فلا شيء مما هو مكون غير فاسد. ثم نقول كل فاسد هو مكوّن ولا شيء مما هو مكوّن غير فاسد ينتج أنه لا شيء مما هو فاسد فغير فاسد. ثم يقال كل شيء لا يخلو من فاسد وغير فاسد، والفاسد وغير الفاسد لا يجتمعان فكل شيء لا يخلو مما لا يجتمعان، وكل شيء لا يخلو مما لا يجتمعان فهو إما هذا وإما ذاك : فكل شيء إذن إما هذا الذي هو الفاسد وإما ذاك. فأحد القياسين يبين أن كل شيء لا يخلو عنهما جميعا، والثاني يبين أن لا شيء من الأشياء يوجد فيه كلاهما.
As for the conversion of mutuals and opposites: if each of 'A' and 'B' is convertible upon the other in predication, and likewise (C) and (D) are such, and (D) and (C) are subject... either the thing 'C' and 'D' absolutely or the more specific, it is inevitable that it be either (C) or (A). Likewise it is 'L B' and 'D' because if when it is found in the subject 'A', B is found in it; and then C is not found in it, so it is necessary that D also not be found in it, otherwise C would be found in it because every C is D. So when B is present in it, D is not present. And also when D is present in it, C must be present in it and they are not present; so it is necessary that B not be present in it, otherwise they would both be present. And I say whichever of them is present, the other is present, because if D is not present in it and B is present in it, because if D is not present then C is not present, otherwise C would be not (B) D. So if C is not present, then A is present in it, and every A is B, so B would be present in it. And likewise the converse. Example: the generated and the corruptible if one of them converts upon the other, then the non-generated and the non-corruptible convert upon each other; and every thing is either generated or non-generated, so every thing is either corruptible or non-corruptible. These are two compound syllogisms: one shows that a thing is described by one of these two, and the second shows that they do not coexist in it together, so from them the required is known. As for the first of them: 'Every thing is either generated or non-generated', and 'Every generated is corruptible', and 'Every non-generated is non-corruptible' produces: 'Every thing is not free from being corruptible or non-corruptible'. But the meaning of 'not free' here is not that they do not coexist in it and do not depart from it, but rather that it is not found separated from both of them, even if their coexistence in it is possible. This itself is a compound syllogism from two syllogisms: one is 'Every thing is either generated or non-generated' and 'Every generated is corruptible' produces that every thing is not free from being either corruptible or non-generated. And if you said 'Every thing is either corruptible or non-generated' and 'Every non-generated is non-corruptible' produces that every thing is either corruptible or non-corruptible, but not in the sense that they cannot coexist in it as before, but it needs another syllogism to complete that. And it is also compound from syllogisms: 'Nothing that is generated is non-generated' and 'Every non-corruptible is non-generated', so nothing that is generated is non-corruptible. Then we say: 'Every corruptible is generated' and 'Nothing that is generated is non-corruptible' produces that nothing that is corruptible is non-corruptible. Then it is said: 'Every thing is not free from corruptible and non-corruptible', and 'corruptible and non-corruptible do not coexist', so every thing is not free from what do not coexist, and every thing that is not free from what do not coexist is either this or that; so every thing is either this which is corruptible or that. So one of the two syllogisms shows that every thing is not free from both of them together, and the second shows that nothing is found in which both of them exist.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a definition and demonstration of the conversion of mutuals and opposites. The author begins by setting up a general principle: if two terms A and B are convertible (i.e., each implies the other in predication), and another pair C and D are also convertible, and there is a relation between the two pairs, then certain implications hold. He then gives a detailed example using the terms 'generated' (مكون) and 'corruptible' (فاسد) and their opposites. The goal is to show that everything is either corruptible or non-corruptible, and that these two cannot coexist. The author constructs two compound syllogisms: the first shows that every thing is covered by one of the two (corruptible or non-corruptible), but allows for the possibility of coexistence; the second proves that they cannot coexist. The first compound syllogism is built from simpler syllogisms: from 'Everything is either generated or non-generated' and 'Every generated is corruptible' we get that everything is either corruptible or non-generated. Then from that and 'Every non-generated is non-corruptible' we get the desired disjunction, but this only shows coverage, not mutual exclusion. To prove exclusion, the author uses a second compound argument: 'Nothing generated is non-generated' and 'Every non-corruptible is non-generated' yield 'Nothing generated is non-corruptible'. Then with 'Every corruptible is generated', we get 'Nothing corruptible is non-corruptible', establishing that the two cannot coexist. Finally, combining coverage and exclusion, we conclude that everything is either exactly one of the two. This lesson illustrates the power of compound syllogisms in demonstrating complex logical relationships typical in Aristotelian physics and metaphysics.

قياس مركب

The Syllogism

Compound Syllogism for Generated and Corruptible
Concrete demonstration of compound syllogisms for generated and corruptible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to deconstruct a compound syllogism into its component syllogisms and verify that it proves both coverage and mutual exclusion of two opposite terms.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism can prove that everything falls under one of two categories and that the categories are mutually exclusive.

The author applies the abstract principle to a concrete example: everything is either generated or non-generated, and generated implies corruptible, non-generated implies non-corruptible. Using two compound syllogisms, he shows that everything is either corruptible or non-corruptible, and that these two cannot coexist. This lesson walks through the steps of these compound arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms and their opposites
The example uses generated (مكوّن) and corruptible (فاسد) and their opposites non-generated (غير مكوّن) and non-corruptible (غير فاسد). Note the conversion: generated implies corruptible, non-generated implies non-corruptible.
2
Analyze the first compound syllogism (coverage)
The first chain uses: (1) Everything is either generated or non-generated. (2) Every generated is corruptible. (3) Every non-generated is non-corruptible. This yields that everything is either corruptible or non-corruptible, but this does not exclude the possibility of something being both (as the author notes).
3
Analyze the second compound syllogism (exclusion)
The second chain uses: (a) Nothing generated is non-generated. (b) Every non-corruptible is non-generated → Nothing generated is non-corruptible. (c) Every corruptible is generated → Nothing corruptible is non-corruptible. This proves that nothing can be both.
4
Combine coverage and exclusion
The final conclusion is that everything is exactly one of the two: corruptible or non-corruptible, and they are mutually exclusive.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and demonstration of the conversion of mutuals and opposites. The author begins by setting up a general principle: if two terms A and B are convertible (i.e., each implies the other in predication), and another pair C and D are also convertible, and there is a relation between the two pairs, then certain implications hold. He then gives a detailed example using the terms 'generated' (مكون) and 'corruptible' (فاسد) and their opposites. The goal is to show that everything is either corruptible or non-corruptible, and that these two cannot coexist. The author constructs two compound syllogisms: the first shows that every thing is covered by one of the two (corruptible or non-corruptible), but allows for the possibility of coexistence; the second proves that they cannot coexist. The first compound syllogism is built from simpler syllogisms: from 'Everything is either generated or non-generated' and 'Every generated is corruptible' we get that everything is either corruptible or non-generated. Then from that and 'Every non-generated is non-corruptible' we get the desired disjunction, but this only shows coverage, not mutual exclusion. To prove exclusion, the author uses a second compound argument: 'Nothing generated is non-generated' and 'Every non-corruptible is non-generated' yield 'Nothing generated is non-corruptible'. Then with 'Every corruptible is generated', we get 'Nothing corruptible is non-corruptible', establishing that the two cannot coexist. Finally, combining coverage and exclusion, we conclude that everything is either exactly one of the two. This lesson illustrates the power of compound syllogisms in demonstrating complex logical relationships typical in Aristotelian physics and metaphysics.

Key Terms
قياس مركب
مكوّن
فاسد

Checkpoint

A) That they are opposites
B) That everything is either corruptible or non-corruptible
C) That nothing can be both corruptible and non-corruptible
D) That everything is either generated or non-generated

Key Takeaway
A rigorous proof of a dichotomy requires two steps: establishing coverage (everything falls under one of the two) and exclusion (nothing falls under both).

Analyzing the Compound Syllogism of Corruptibility
Author explains the first compound syllogism showing coverage of corruptible and non-corruptible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decompose a compound syllogism into its component syllogisms and identify their intermediate conclusions.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms chain simple syllogisms to reach conclusions beyond a single argument.

The author presents a compound syllogism that proves everything is either corruptible or not corruptible. He then breaks it down into two linked syllogisms and clarifies a subtle point about the phrase 'not free from'. This lesson will guide you through the structure of this compound syllogism and its logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall conclusion
The final conclusion of the compound syllogism is 'every thing is not free from being either corruptible or not corruptible'. Note the phrase 'not free from' (لا يخلو).
2
Find the first sub-syllogism
The author decomposes it: first syllogism: 'Everything is either generated or not generated. Every generated is corruptible. Therefore, everything is either corruptible or not generated.' This conclusion becomes a premise for the next syllogism.
This uses a disjunctive major premise.
3
Find the second sub-syllogism
Second syllogism: 'Everything is either corruptible or not generated. Every not generated is not corruptible. Therefore, everything is corruptible or not corruptible.'
The conclusion matches the overall conclusion but without the 'not free from' nuance.
4
Interpret the nuance of 'not free from'
The author clarifies that 'not free from' does not mean the two attributes (corruptible and not corruptible) cannot coexist. Rather, it means the thing is never devoid of both simultaneously. Coexistence is possible (e.g., something could be both generated and corruptible? Actually, generated things are corruptible, so they have both attributes? Wait, corruptible and not corruptible are contradictory? Actually, the text notes that the conclusion does not preclude coexistence; to prove non-coexistence another syllogism is needed. This nuances the logical force.
This shows awareness of the difference between 'not both absent' and 'mutually exclusive'.

فأما الأول منهما فهو إن كل شيء إما مكوّن وإما غير مكوّن وكل مكوّن فاسد، وكل غير مكوّن غير فاسد ينتج كل شيء لا يخلو عن كونه فاسدا أو غير فاسد. ولكن معنى لا يخلو هنا ليس هو أنه لا يجتمعان فيه ولا يزولان عنه بل إنه لا يوجد مفارقًا لهما جميعًا وإن جاز اجتماعهما فيه. وهذا في نفسه قياس مركب من قياسين أحدهما أن كل شيء إما مكون وإما غير مكوّن، وكل مكوّن فاسد ينتج أن كل شيء لا يخلو من أن يكون فاسدًا أو غير مكوّن. ولو قلت كل شيء إما فاسد وإما غير مكوّن، وكل غير مكون غير فاسد ينتج أن كل شيء إما فاسد وإما غير فاسد ولكن ليس على معنى أنه لا يمكن اجتماعهما فيه كما مرَّ بل يفتقر في إتمام ذلك إلى قياس آخر.
As for the first of them, it is that every thing is either generated or not generated, and every generated is corruptible, and every non-generated is not corruptible, so it yields that every thing is not free from being either corruptible or not corruptible. But the meaning of 'not free from' here is not that they do not coexist in it and do not depart from it, but rather that it is not found separate from both of them, even if their coexistence in it is possible. And this in itself is a compound syllogism of two syllogisms: one of them is that every thing is either generated or not generated, and every generated is corruptible, yields that every thing is not free from being either corruptible or not generated. And if you said: every thing is either corruptible or not generated, and every not generated is not corruptible, yields that every thing is either corruptible or not corruptible, but not in the sense that their coexistence in it is impossible, as mentioned, rather it requires another syllogism to complete that.

Commentary
This passage explains the first compound syllogism that proves everything is either corruptible or not corruptible. The author begins by presenting the syllogism: every thing is either generated or non-generated; every generated is corruptible; every non-generated is not corruptible; therefore, every thing is not free from being either corruptible or not corruptible. He then clarifies that the phrase 'not free from' does not mean that the two attributes cannot coexist, but rather that the thing is never devoid of both simultaneously—it may have both. The author then breaks this syllogism into a compound of two. The first sub-syllogism: everything is either generated or non-generated, every generated is corruptible, so everything is either corruptible or non-generated. Then, using that conclusion as a premise, if we take 'everything is either corruptible or non-generated' and 'every non-generated is not corruptible', we get 'everything is either corruptible or not corruptible'. However, this conclusion does not preclude coexistence; to show non-coexistence, another syllogism is needed. In the Islamic context, 'generated' (mukawwan) refers to created things, 'corruptible' (fasad) to those that decay, while 'non-generated' and 'non-corruptible' refer to the Creator. This syllogism establishes that every entity falls into one of these categories, but further proof is needed for mutual exclusivity.

Deconstructing a Compound Syllogism on Corruption and Eternity
Author constructs a second compound syllogism proving corruptible and non-corruptible cannot coexist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to break down the compound syllogism in the passage into its constituent syllogisms and verify that the conclusion 'everything is either corruptible or non-corruptible' logically follows.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism links multiple syllogisms to prove a conclusion that no single syllogism could reach alone.

This passage presents a sophisticated logical argument using a compound syllogism to prove that corruptible and non-corruptible cannot coexist. In Islamic theology, this distinction underlies the difference between the temporal created world and the eternal Creator. By analyzing this argument step by step, you will learn how compound syllogisms work and how they build certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main conclusion
The passage aims to prove that everything is either corruptible (fāsid) or non-corruptible (ghayr fāsid), and nothing can be both. Locate this final statement.
Look for the phrase 'فكل شيء إذن إما هذا الذي هو الفاسد وإما ذاك'.
2
Break down the first component syllogism
The first part: 'nothing generated is non-generated; every non-corruptible is non-generated; therefore nothing generated is non-corruptible'. Write it in standard categorical form.
Major premise: 'every non-corruptible is non-generated'; Minor premise: 'nothing generated is non-generated'; Conclusion: 'nothing generated is non-corruptible'.
3
Identify the second component syllogism
Every corruptible is generated; from step 2, nothing generated is non-corruptible; therefore nothing corruptible is non-corruptible.
The conclusion of the first syllogism becomes a premise here.
4
Combine the results
The two syllogisms show that (a) everything is either generated or non-generated (implied), and (b) corruptible and non-corruptible are mutually exclusive. Then the final reasoning uses the principle that if two things are mutually exclusive and everything falls under them, then everything is one or the other.
The passage uses 'كل شيء لا يخلو من فاسد وغير فاسد' and 'الفاسد وغير الفاسد لا يجتمعان' to derive the final dichotomy.
5
Verify the compound structure
Note that the conclusion of the first syllogism becomes a premise in the second. This linkage is what makes it a compound syllogism.
Compound syllogisms chain intermediate conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو أيضًا مركب من قياسات وهو أنه لا شيء مما هو مكوّن فغير مكوّن وكل غير فاسد غير مكوّن فلا شيء مما هو مكون غير فاسد. ثم نقول كل فاسد هو مكوّن ولا شيء مما هو مكوّن غير فاسد ينتج أنه لا شيء مما هو فاسد فغير فاسد. ثم يقال كل شيء لا يخلو من فاسد وغير فاسد، والفاسد وغير الفاسد لا يجتمعان فكل شيء لا يخلو مما لا يجتمعان، وكل شيء لا يخلو مما لا يجتمعان فهو إما هذا وإما ذاك : فكل شيء إذن إما هذا الذي هو الفاسد وإما ذاك. فأحد القياسين يبين أن كل شيء لا يخلو عنهما جميعا، والثاني يبين أن لا شيء من الأشياء يوجد فيه كلاهما.
And it is also compound of syllogisms: that nothing that is generated is non-generated, and every non-corruptible is non-generated, so nothing that is generated is non-corruptible. Then we say: every corruptible is generated, and nothing that is generated is non-corruptible, yields that nothing that is corruptible is non-corruptible. Then it is said: every thing is not free from corruptible and non-corruptible, and corruptible and non-corruptible do not coexist, so every thing is not free from what do not coexist, and every thing not free from what do not coexist is either this or that: so every thing is either this, which is corruptible, or that. So one of the two syllogisms shows that every thing is not free from both together, and the second shows that nothing exists in which both are found.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage explains the second compound syllogism that demonstrates the mutual exclusivity of corruptible and non-corruptible. The author builds a chain: first, nothing that is generated is non-generated, and every non-corruptible is non-generated, so nothing that is generated is non-corruptible. Then, every corruptible is generated, and nothing generated is non-corruptible, so nothing corruptible is non-corruptible. Then, using the earlier result that everything is not free from the two, and given that they do not coexist, it follows that everything is either corruptible or non-corruptible exclusively. The author concludes that one syllogism shows coverage (everything is not free from both), and the other shows exclusivity (nothing has both). In Islamic theology, this supports the distinction between the temporal (generated and corruptible) and the eternal (non-generated and non-corruptible).

Key Terms

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism builds certainty by using intermediate conclusions as premises, allowing the logician to prove conclusions that would otherwise be inaccessible.

Conditional Conjunctive Syllogisms with Four Terms
Author illustrates conditional conjunctive syllogism using four terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate a conditional conjunctive syllogism with four terms using reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Complex conditional syllogisms can be proven via reductio and term convertibility.

This passage demonstrates an advanced conditional conjunctive syllogism involving four terms. It shows how, given two disjunctions and a convertibility between two terms, one can infer a similar convertibility between the other two. The proof uses reductio ad absurdum, a fundamental logical tool. For students of Islamic logic, this example extends the basic conditional syllogism to more complex structures found in later commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Extract the two disjunctions: every subject has either A or B, and either C or D. Also note the convertibility: every A is C and every C is A.
The subject is the underlying entity, like an individual in the domain.
2
State the desired conclusion
The goal is to prove that every B is D and every D is B—that is, B and D are convertible.
The conclusion mirrors the given convertibility between A and C.
3
Assume the opposite for reductio
Assume that some D is not B. This contradicts the desired conclusion. Then, since every subject has A or B, that D must have A.
The disjunction forces that if not B, then A.
4
Derive a contradiction
If some D is A and every A is C, then some D is C. But the disjunction says the subject has C or D, so D and C cannot coexist. This is a contradiction (خلاف).
The contradiction proves the assumption false, so every D is B. The proof for 'every B is D' is symmetric.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن لم يعلم الأقيسة الشرطية الاقترانية فإنه يهتم في بيان هذا. وأيضًا إن كان كل الموضوع إما أن يوجد فيه «أ» وإما «ب». وأيضًا إما أن يوجد فيه «ج» وإما «د». ثمَّ كان كل (أ) (ج) وكل (ج) (أ) فكل (ب) (د) وكل (د) (ب). وإلا فليكن بعض (د) ليس (ب) فيكون (أ) لأنه لا يخلو الموضوع عن «أ» و»ب». وإذا كان بعض (د) (أ) وكل (أ) (ج) فبعض (د) (ج) وكان «د» و «ج» لا يجتمعان؛ هذا خلف.
And whoever does not know conditional conjunctive syllogisms will be perplexed in explaining this. Also, if every subject either has A or B, and also either has C or D, then if every A is C and every C is A, then every B is D and every D is B. Otherwise, let some D not be B, then it has A, because the subject is not free from A and B. And if some D is A and every A is C, then some D is C, and D and C do not coexist; this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage illustrates a conditional conjunctive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil iqtirani) using placeholder terms A, B, C, D. The author states that if every subject has either A or B, and also either C or D (disjunctions), and if A and C are convertible (every A is C and every C is A), then B and D are also convertible. The proof uses reductio: assume some D is not B. Then that D must have A (since every subject has A or B). But that D has A, and every A is C, so that D is C. However, D and C cannot coexist because they are in a disjunction? The text says 'D and C do not coexist'—likely meaning they are mutually exclusive in the original disjunction (since subject has either C or D). This yields a contradiction, so the assumption is false, hence every D is B. Similarly for the converse. This form of reasoning is used in complex logical problems.

Immediate Inferences from Opposed Terms
Author demonstrates categorical conversions with four terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive categorical conversions involving four terms based on opposition and co-extensiveness.

Core Idea
Opposites reflect the logical relations between their counterparts.

The passage begins with a setup: the subject contains either A or B, and either C or D. This creates a framework for deriving relationships between these terms. We will focus on how, given that A and C are co-extensive (every A is C and every C is A), we can conclude that B and D are also co-extensive. This uses the logical principle of contraposition through opposites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four terms and their opposites
Note that A is opposite to B, and C is opposite to D. This means A and B cannot both be true of the same subject, nor both false; similarly for C and D.
2
State the given co-extensiveness
The passage assumes: every A is C and every C is A. That is, A and C are identical in extension.
3
Apply the principle of opposites
If every A is C, then whatever is not C is not A. But 'not C' is equivalent to D (since the subject has either C or D, and D is the opposite of C). Similarly, 'not A' is equivalent to B. Therefore, every D is B. By the same reasoning, every B is D.
This is a form of contraposition using the exclusive disjunction.
4
Consider the alternative case
The passage also says: if the co-extensiveness is false, a contradiction arises. This shows the necessity of the conclusion.
The author uses a subclass argument to show that if some D is not B, then it would be A, leading to a contradiction with the exclusivity of C and D.

Source Text

And whoever does not know conditional conjunctive syllogisms will struggle in explaining this. Also, if the entire subject either contains 'A' or 'B', and also either contains 'C' or 'D'. Then if every A is C and every C is A, then every B is D and every D is B. Otherwise, let some D not be B, so it would be A because the subject is not devoid of A and B. And if some D is A and every A is C, then some D is C, but D and C cannot coexist; this is a contradiction. And if A exists in every B and in every C only, and does not exist in others, then if every C is B, then every A is B because B is predicated of everything of which A is predicated, since A is predicated only of B and C. Then B is predicated of B and of C, so B is predicated of everything of which A is predicated, so every B is A. If 'every B is C' is converted, then 'every A is B' is also converted; this is evident. Also, if every C is A and every C is B, and every B is C, then every B is A because every B is C and every C is A. And we say: let A and D be desired, and C and B be avoided, and A and B are opposites. So we say: if A and C combined are better than B and D combined, then A is better than D. That is because A is desired just as B is avoided because they are opposites. Likewise, C is desired just as D is avoided because they are opposites. If A is not better than D, then either it is equal to D, or D is better. But if it is equal to D in being desired, then their opposites must be equal in being avoided, meaning the opposite of C for A and the opposite of B for A. So if C is added to A, a desired and an avoided are combined. And if B is added to D, a desired and an avoided are combined. And the sum of those two in desire and avoidance equals the sum of these two, so the combination of A and C is not superior to the combination of B and D, yet it was superior; this is a contradiction. Or if we say that D is better than A in being a preferred desired, then the opposite of D, which is extremely opposite to it, is more in the avoidance category because the lesser corresponds to the lesser, and the greater to the greater. So then C is more to be avoided than B, so B is more preferred than C. Then B and D together are more preferred than A and C. But it is not so. So consider the rulings mentioned in the materials, for they have examples.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents a complex logical proof concerning conditional conjunctive syllogisms and a comparative preference argument. The author begins by emphasizing the difficulty of understanding this material for those unfamiliar with conditional conjunctive syllogisms (al-aqīsah al-sharṭiyyah al-iqtirāniyyah). The proof uses four variables (A, B, C, D) representing predicates or terms. The author sets up a scenario: the subject contains either A or B, and either C or D. He then explores implications using categorical syllogistic rules. For instance, if every A is C and every C is A, then every B is D and every D is B. If not, a contradiction arises via a subclass. The author also examines cases where A is limited to B and C, and demonstrates conversions of propositions. The main argument concerns preference (afḍaliyyah). Let A and D be desired (maṭlūb), and B and C be avoided (mahrūb ʿanhu), with A and B being opposites (mutaqābilān). The claim is: if the combination of A and C is better than the combination of B and D, then A is better than D. The proof proceeds by reductio ad absurdum (khalaf). If A is not better than D, then either equality or D better. The author shows that both lead to contradictions, establishing the conclusion. The passage ends by advising the reader to consider the mentioned rulings in the materials, as they have examples. This proof exemplifies the application of categorical syllogistic rules to comparative judgments, a technique seen in later Islamic logic treatises.

The Syllogism

10 min
Reductio Proof for Comparative Preference
Author proves a preference claim via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a reductio ad absurdum proof for a preference claim using categorical reasoning about desired and avoided terms.

Core Idea
To prove one thing is better than another, assume the opposite and derive a contradiction.

The author presents a sophisticated proof: if the combination of A and C is better than B and D, then A alone is better than D. Here A and D are desired (maṭlūb), while B and C are avoided (mahrūb ʿanhu), with A opposite to B and C opposite to D. The proof uses a reductio (khalaf) and relies on the idea that equal desire requires equal avoidance of opposites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms and their values
A and D are desired (positive), B and C are avoided (negative). A is opposite to B, C is opposite to D.
This sets up the comparative judgments.
2
State the claim to be proved
Claim: If the combination A+C is better (afḍal) than B+D, then A is better than D.
3
Assume the opposite of the conclusion
Assume that A is not better than D. Then either A is equal to D, or D is better than A.
4
Derive a contradiction from equality
If A equals D in desire, then their opposites (B and C) must be equal in avoidance. Then A+C and B+D are equal in combined value, contradicting the given that A+C is better.
The author says: 'the sum of those two in desire and avoidance equals the sum of these two, so the combination of A and C is not superior... yet it was superior; this is a contradiction.'
5
Derive a contradiction from D being better
If D is better than A, then its opposite C is more avoided than B (since greater desire implies greater avoidance of opposite). This leads to B being more desired than C, so B+D becomes better than A+C, again contradicting the given.
This step uses the principle that the more desired something is, the more its opposite is avoided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And whoever does not know conditional conjunctive syllogisms will struggle in explaining this. Also, if the entire subject either contains 'A' or 'B', and also either contains 'C' or 'D'. Then if every A is C and every C is A, then every B is D and every D is B. Otherwise, let some D not be B, so it would be A because the subject is not devoid of A and B. And if some D is A and every A is C, then some D is C, but D and C cannot coexist; this is a contradiction. And if A exists in every B and in every C only, and does not exist in others, then if every C is B, then every A is B because B is predicated of everything of which A is predicated, since A is predicated only of B and C. Then B is predicated of B and of C, so B is predicated of everything of which A is predicated, so every B is A. If 'every B is C' is converted, then 'every A is B' is also converted; this is evident. Also, if every C is A and every C is B, and every B is C, then every B is A because every B is C and every C is A. And we say: let A and D be desired, and C and B be avoided, and A and B are opposites. So we say: if A and C combined are better than B and D combined, then A is better than D. Three Types of Qiyas: Fiqhī, Ta'aqqulī, Khaṭabī
Classification of qiyas into fiqhī, ta'aqqulī, khaṭabī

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish fiqhī, ta'aqqulī, and khaṭabī syllogisms based on their purpose, structure, and authoritative basis.

Core Idea
Syllogisms are classified by their domain of use: legal reasoning, intellectual proof, or rhetorical persuasion.

This passage from a classical logic text breaks down qiyas into three practical categories: juridical analogies (fiqhī), intellectual syllogisms (ta'aqqulī), and rhetorical arguments (khaṭabī). Each type serves a different function, from establishing legal rulings to compelling opponents in debate. Understanding this classification helps you recognise which logical tools are appropriate in different Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of classification
The author classifies syllogisms by their use, not by their form. Notice that he says they 'may be called fiqhī, ta'aqqulī, or khaṭabī' depending on what they are related to.
This is a functional classification, not a formal one.
2
Analyse the khaṭabī (rhetorical) type
Look at the three subcategories: enthymemes (ḍamā'ir) with missing major premise, examples (mithālāt) that are probably true, and probable compositions. Their purpose is to persuade or compel an opponent, often about particular matters.
Khaṭabī arguments are common in debate and public speech.
3
Analyse the fiqhī (juridical) type
Note the key elements: a precedent (aṣl) taken from the Lawgiver, Companions, or consensus; a common cause (ʿillah); and a transfer of ruling. The author also describes how the Lawgiver's statements may be particular or universal in four combinations.
Fiqhī qiyas is the standard legal analogy in uṣūl al-fiqh.
4
Analyse the ta'aqqulī (intellectual) type
What distinguishes it? It aims at rational understanding (yaqīn) and differs from deliberative (mashwriyyah) and rhetorical syllogisms. It seeks certainty through demonstration.
Ta'aqqulī syllogisms are used in kalām and philosophy.
5
Compare the three types side by side
Create a mental or written contrast: fiqhī relies on authoritative precedent, khaṭabī uses probable premises for persuasion, ta'aqqulī aims at certitude through rational demonstration.
This classification helps you choose the right argumentative strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[ أ ] أما الخطبية فإنها [١] إما ضمائر حذفت فيها الكبريات؛ فإذا ردّت عادت إلى شكل من الأشكال، [٢] وإما مثالات مظنونة الصدق غير معتقد به، [٣] أو مظنونة الإنتاج في التأليف غير معتقد به سواء كانت صادقة أو كاذبة. ولكنها معدة نحو إلزام الخصم أو إقناع جماعته من الحاضرين؛ وأكثرها في الأمور الجزئية. [ب] وأما الفقهية / فإنها أقيسة مثالية وهي التي يحكم فيها على سبب حكم موجود في شبيه المأخوذ [١] من صاحب الشريعة صلوات الله عليه [٢] أو من الصحابة الكرام، [٣] أو هو المتفق عليه مما يرجع إلى المأخوذ عنه. ويسمى الشبيه أصلا، وما يتشابهان به معني وعلة، وما ينتقل من الشبيه إلى شبيهه حكمًا. وقول صاحب الشرع إما جزئي يقوم مقام كلي، وإما كلي يقوم مقام جزئي، وإما جزئي يقوم مقام جزئي، وإما كلي يقوم مقام كلي. فإن الخطاب قد يكون خاصا ويراد به العام، وقد يكون عاما ويراد به الخاص. والكلام في مثل هذا الأقيسة يجيء من بعد. [ت] وأما التعقلية فهي التي تؤلف على إنتاج ما ينبغي أن يعقل ويخالف [ث] المشورية بما تخالف به الخطبية. فإن الخطبية جماع الأمر فيها أن يكون على سبيل المخاطبة فلا يقال لمن
Section: On fiqhī and ta'aqqulī syllogisms. As for the syllogisms that have been mentioned previously, from conjunctive and others, they may be called fiqhī, and may be called ta'aqqulī, and may be called khaṭabī; and similarly with respect to what they are related to. [A] As for the khaṭabī (rhetorical), they are: [1] either enthymemes in which the major premises have been omitted; when restored, they return to one of the figures; [2] or examples whose truth is probable but not believed with certainty; [3] or [syllogisms] whose production in composition is probable but not believed with certainty, whether true or false. But they are prepared towards compelling the opponent or persuading his group of attendees; and most of them are about particular matters. [B] As for the fiqhī (juridical), they are exemplary syllogisms, and they are those in which one judges based on a cause of a ruling present in a similar precedent taken from the Lawgiver (peace be upon him), or from the noble Companions, or it is what is agreed upon that goes back to the one from whom it is taken. The similar is called the original (aṣl), and that by which they are similar is the meaning and cause (ʿillah), and what is transferred from the similar to its similar is the ruling. And the statement of the Lawgiver is either particular standing in place of universal, or universal standing in place of particular, or particular standing in place of particular, or universal standing in place of universal. For speech may be specific and intended as general, and may be general and intended as specific. The discussion of such syllogisms comes later. [C] As for the ta'aqqulī (intellectual), they are those composed to produce what should be rationally understood and differ from the deliberative (mashwriyyah) in how they differ from the rhetorical. For the rhetorical, its whole affair is that it is by way of address, so one does not say to...
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a classification section (faṣl) from a logic work, defining three types of qiyas (syllogisms) based on their application: fiqhī (juridical), ta'aqqulī (intellectual), and khaṭabī (rhetorical). The author begins by stating that the syllogisms previously discussed (conjunctive and others) can be named according to their domain. He then details each type. First, the khaṭabī syllogisms are used for persuasion or compelling an opponent in debate. They come in three subcategories: (1) enthymemes (ḍamā'ir) where the major premise is omitted but can be restored to a standard figure; (2) examples (mithālāt) that are probably true but not certain; (3) compositions that are probably productive but not certain, regardless of truth value. These are common in particular matters. Second, the fiqhī syllogisms are analogical reasoning used in Islamic jurisprudence. They are based on a precedent (shabīh) taken from the Lawgiver (the Prophet, his Companions, or consensus). The precedent is called the original (aṣl), the common attribute is the meaning and cause (ʿillah), and the transfer is of the ruling. The author notes that the Lawgiver's statement can take four forms regarding particularity and universality, and that the same wording may be interpreted as general or specific. This will be elaborated later. Third, the ta'aqqulī syllogisms are intellectual, aiming to produce what should be rationally understood. They differ from deliberative (mashwriyyah) and rhetorical syllogisms. The passage ends abruptly, noting that rhetorical syllogisms are by way of address. This classification helps the logician understand the purpose and structure of arguments in different fields.

Key Terms
قياس فقهي (qiyās fiqhī)
قياس تعقلي (qiyās ta'aqqulī)
قياس خطبي (qiyās khaṭabī)
ضمائر (ḍamā'ir)
مثالات (mithālāt)
أصل (aṣl)
علة (ʿillah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A rhetorical syllogism using probable premises or enthymemes for persuasion

Key Takeaway
The classification of syllogisms by domain of use helps the logician apply the correct reasoning method in Islamic law, intellectual discourse, or debate.

Rhetorical Syllogisms: Definition and Types
Author defines rhetorical syllogisms and lists their three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of rhetorical syllogisms and explain their persuasive purpose.

Core Idea
Rhetorical syllogisms persuade or refute; they don't aim at certainty.

After studying the formal structure of categorical and conditional syllogisms, we now turn to rhetorical syllogisms. These are not defined by form but by purpose: persuasion and refutation. The author classifies them into three types, each with a distinct logical feature. Understanding these types helps you analyze arguments in kalam and fiqh debates where certainty is not required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the broader classification
Notice that the author first mentions three terms: juridical (fiqhiyya), rational (ta'aqquliyya), and rhetorical (khaṭabiyya). These are not mutually exclusive classes but rather perspectives on the same syllogisms.
The passage says these names apply depending on what the syllogism is related to.
2
Focus on the rhetorical type
The author then defines rhetorical syllogisms as those used for persuasion or refutation. They are not aimed at certain knowledge but at influencing an audience.
The purpose distinguishes them from demonstrative syllogisms.
3
Analyze the first subtype: enthymemes (ḍamā'ir)
Enthymemes are syllogisms where the major premise is omitted. When you supply the missing premise, they conform to one of the standard figures.
This makes them formally valid but pragmatically incomplete.
4
Analyze the second and third subtypes
Both are called 'examples' (mithālāt). The second are examples believed to be true but not certain; the third are examples believed to yield a probable conclusion regardless of truth. Both rely on probability, not certainty.
The author says they are not held as certain ('ghayr mu'taqad bih').
5
Synthesize the purpose
All three types serve to refute an opponent or persuade an audience, and they mostly deal with particular matters. This shows their practical, case-based nature.
Contrast with universal demonstrative syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في القياسات الفقهية والتعقلية. أما الأقيسة التي مرَّ ذكرها من الاقترانيات وغيرها فإنها قد تسمى فقهية، وقد تسمَّى تعقلية، وقد تسمّى خطبية؛ وكذلك بالنسبة إلى ما هي متعلقة به. [ أ ] أما الخطبية فإنها [١] إما ضمائر حذفت فيها الكبريات؛ فإذا ردّت عادت إلى شكل من الأشكال، [٢] وإما مثالات مظنونة الصدق غير معتقد به، [٣] أو مظنونة الإنتاج في التأليف غير معتقد به سواء كانت صادقة أو كاذبة. ولكنها معدة نحو إلزام الخصم أو إقناع جماعته من الحاضرين؛ وأكثرها في الأمور الجزئية.
Chapter: On Juridical and Rational Syllogisms. As for the syllogisms that have been mentioned previously, including conjunctive and others, they may be called juridical, rational, or rhetorical, depending on what they are related to. [A] As for rhetorical syllogisms, they are either [1] enthymemes in which the major premises are omitted, such that when restored they revert to one of the figures; [2] examples that are believed to be true but not held as certain; [3] or examples believed to produce a conclusion in composition but not held as certain, whether they are true or false. They are prepared for refuting an opponent or persuading his audience of those present, and most of them are about particular matters.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of syllogisms into three types: juridical (fiqhiyya), rational (ta'aqquliyya), and rhetorical (khatabiyya). The author first states that the previously discussed syllogisms (conjunctive and conditional) can be categorized under these names. Then he focuses on rhetorical syllogisms, defining them as those used for persuasion or refutation rather than for certain knowledge. He lists three subtypes: (1) enthymemes where the major premise is omitted and when supplied, the syllogism fits one of the figures; (2) examples considered probable but not certain; (3) examples considered to yield probable conclusions, regardless of truth or falsity. The purpose is to refute an opponent or persuade an audience, and most concern particular matters. This highlights the pragmatic nature of rhetoric in contrast to demonstration.

Key Terms
القياسات الخطبية
ضمائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary purpose of rhetorical (khaṭabiyya) syllogisms?
A) To produce certain knowledge
B) To refute an opponent or persuade an audience
C) To define terms precisely
D) To induce general laws from particulars

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical syllogisms are defined by their persuasive purpose, not by formal validity, and they come in three types: enthymemes, probable examples, and compositionally plausible examples.

?

Juridical Syllogisms Defined and Classified
Author defines juridical syllogisms and classifies the original source statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define juridical syllogisms and classify the four types of Lawgiver statements.

Core Idea
Juridical syllogisms are analogical, not categorical, transferring rulings from a root case.

This lesson introduces the juridical syllogism (qiyas fiqhi), a cornerstone of Islamic legal reasoning. Unlike categorical syllogisms, these are analogical arguments that extend a ruling from an original case (aṣl) to a new case (farʿ) based on a shared effective cause (ʿilla). The passage also reveals how the Lawgiver's statements vary in particularity and universality, a crucial insight for proper legal interpretation. Let us parse the definition and its components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of juridical syllogism
Locate the phrase 'أما الفقهية فإنها أقيسة مثالية' and note that the author defines them as analogical syllogisms, not categorical ones.
This sets the genus: they belong to analogical reasoning.
2
Extract the three components: aṣl, ʿilla, and judgment transfer
The passage states that judgment is made 'on the basis of a cause present in a similar case' and that the similar case is called aṣl, the shared meaning/cause is ʿilla, and the judgment is transferred.
Memorize these terms: aṣl (root), ʿilla (cause), and the transfer (naql) of the ruling.
3
Recognize the sources of the root case
The root can come from the Lawgiver (Prophet), the noble Companions, or consensus (ijmāʿ). Identify these as authoritative sources for the analogy.
This distinguishes juridical syllogisms from ordinary analogies.
4
Classify the four types of Lawgiver statements
The passage lists four possible relationships between particular (juzʾī) and universal (kullī) in the Lawgiver's statement. Understand each: particular standing for universal (e.g., a specific verse implying a general rule), universal for particular, etc.
This classification is essential for interpreting scriptural texts correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ب] وأما الفقهية فإنها أقيسة مثالية وهي التي يحكم فيها على سبب حكم موجود في شبيه المأخوذ من صاحب الشريعة صلوات الله عليه أو من الصحابة الكرام، أو هو المتفق عليه مما يرجع إلى المأخوذ عنه. ويسمى الشبيه أصلا، وما يتشابهان به معني وعلة، وما ينتقل من الشبيه إلى شبيهه حكمًا. وقول صاحب الشرع إما جزئي يقوم مقام كلي، وإما كلي يقوم مقام جزئي، وإما جزئي يقوم مقام جزئي، وإما كلي يقوم مقام كلي. فإن الخطاب قد يكون خاصا ويراد به العام، وقد يكون عاما ويراد به الخاص. والكلام في مثل هذا الأقيسة يجيء من بعد.
[B] As for juridical syllogisms, they are analogical syllogisms in which judgment is made on the basis of a cause present in a similar case taken from the Lawgiver (peace be upon him) or from the noble Companions, or it is a consensus that goes back to the taken-from. The similar case is called the root (aṣl), and what they share in terms of meaning and cause (ʿilla), and the judgment is transferred from the similar to its similar. The statement of the Lawgiver is either particular that stands in the place of a universal, or universal that stands in the place of a particular, or particular that stands in the place of a particular, or universal that stands in the place of a universal. For the address may be specific but intended to be general, or general but intended to be specific. The discussion of such syllogisms will come later.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage defines juridical syllogisms (fiqhiyya) as analogical reasoning used in Islamic jurisprudence. The author explains that these syllogisms base judgment on a cause (ʿilla) found in an original case (the root, aṣl) derived from the Lawgiver (Prophet or Companions) or from consensus. The original case and the new case share a common meaning (maʿnā) and cause. The statement of the Lawgiver can have four relationships between particular and universal: a particular standing for a universal, a universal for a particular, etc., because legislative statements may be specific but imply general application, or general but apply to specific cases. The author notes that a detailed discussion will follow later, indicating that this is an introductory definition.

Key Takeaway
Juridical syllogisms transfer a ruling based on a shared cause from a root case to a similar case, and Lawgiver statements can be particular or universal in scope.

Distinguishing Rational Syllogisms from Deliberative and Political Syllogisms
Author defines rational syllogisms and distinguishes them from deliberative and political.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given syllogism as rational, deliberative, or political based on its purpose and premise type.

Core Idea
Rational syllogisms seek truth; deliberative and political seek persuasion.

In Islamic logic, not all syllogisms are built the same. Some are for your own reasoning, others for convincing others. The author here distinguishes three types: rational, deliberative, and political. Understanding the difference helps you know whether an argument aims at truth or at action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the syllogism
Ask: Is this reasoning done by oneself to reach a conclusion, or is it addressed to someone else? Rational syllogisms are for personal deliberation; deliberative and political are for communication.
The author says rational syllogisms are 'composed to produce what should be rationally understood' for oneself.
2
Check the quality of the premises
Rational syllogisms use premises that are true or mostly true in reality. Deliberative and political syllogisms use premises acceptable to the listener, even if not certain.
The passage: 'rational syllogisms are taken from premises that are true or mostly true' vs. the others require 'acceptable to the listener and probable'.
3
Determine the scope
Political syllogisms are a subset: they deal specifically with actions that benefit or harm the city's governance. Rational syllogisms cover that and more specific personal matters.
The author states 'rational syllogisms are more general than political syllogisms'.
4
Classify accordingly
Based on purpose, premise quality, and scope, decide which category the syllogism falls into.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ت] وأما التعقلية فهي التي تؤلف على إنتاج ما ينبغي أن يعقل ويخالف [ث] المشورية بما تخالف به الخطبية. فإن الخطبية جماع الأمر فيها أن يكون على سبيل المخاطبة فلا يقال لمن فكر مع نفسه في إيثار ما يجب أن يفعل ولا يفعل أنه يخاطب. وكذلك يشبه أن المشورية تكون على الغير، ولذلك كانت القياسات التعقلية مأخوذة من مقدمات صادقة أو أكثرية في الحقيقة. وأما الخطبية والمشورية فليس الشرط فيها ذلك بل أن تكون مقبولة عند السامع مظنونة تلزمها النتيجة. وذلك لأن الإنسان ليس غرضه في نفسه أن يلزمها بل أن يهديها إلى الحق وغرضه مع غيره قد يكون الهداية وقد يكون الإلزام. والتعقلية أعم من [ج] السياسية؛ إذ السياسية إنما تكون فيما ينتج ما ينتفع أن يفعل، وترك من الأمور المتعلقة بالمشاركة العائد نفعها إلى تدبير المدينة من حيث هو تدبيرها، والتعقلية تكون في ذلك وفيما هو الأخص من ذلك.
[T] As for rational syllogisms, they are composed to produce what should be rationally understood, and they differ from deliberative syllogisms in the same way that rhetorical differ from deliberative. For the sum of rhetorical is that it is in the mode of address, so one does not say to someone who thinks to himself about preferring what must be done or not done that he is addressing. Similarly, it seems that deliberative syllogisms are for others, and therefore rational syllogisms are taken from premises that are true or mostly true in reality. As for rhetorical and deliberative syllogisms, the condition is not that, but rather that they be acceptable to the listener and probable such that the conclusion follows. This is because man's purpose in himself is not to compel but to guide to truth, and his purpose with others may be guidance or compulsion. Rational syllogisms are more general than [J] political syllogisms, for political syllogisms are only in what produces that it is beneficial to do or abstain from matters related to partnership whose benefit returns to the governance of the city insofar as it is governance, while rational syllogisms are in that and in what is more specific than that.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage defines rational syllogisms (ta'aqquliyya) and compares them to deliberative (shuriyya) and political (siyasiyya) syllogisms. The author explains that rational syllogisms use true or mostly true premises and are used for personal reasoning about what must be understood, contrasting with rhetorical and deliberative syllogisms which rely on acceptability and probability for the audience. Rational syllogisms are more general than political ones, which deal specifically with actions beneficial or harmful to civic governance. The author emphasizes that rational syllogisms are for the individual's own reasoning, while deliberative and rhetorical syllogisms involve addressing others.

Key Terms
القياسات التعقلية
المشورية
السياسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Rational syllogisms are for personal understanding using true premises, while deliberative and political syllogisms are for guiding others using acceptable premises.

Hortatory and Mediating Syllogisms
Author defines hortatory and mediating syllogisms and their premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish hortatory syllogisms from mediating syllogisms by identifying their respective premise compositions.

Core Idea
Two unique syllogism types that blend fiqh with politics or universal praise.

The author goes beyond the standard syllogistic forms to introduce practical syllogisms used in Islamic governance and ethics. Hortatory syllogisms combine fiqh and political premises, while mediating syllogisms mix fiqh with universal praiseworthy opinions. This lesson will help you parse these definitions and see how they function.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate the terms 'حثية' and 'وساطية' in the Arabic. These are the syllogism types being defined.
The passage states 'القياسات الحثية' and 'الوساطية'.
2
Extract premise sources for hortatory
Hortatory syllogisms have general premises from juridical (fiqh) and specific premises from political (siyasa) sources.
عاماتها من المقدمات الفقهية، وخاصاتها من المقدمات السياسية.
3
Extract premise sources for mediating
Mediating syllogisms use mixed premises from juridical and praiseworthy opinions that are not specific to any religious community.
مقدمات مخلوطة مأخوذة من الفقهية والآراء المحمودة التي لا اختصاص لها بملة ملة.
4
Compare the two types
Hortatory uses political specifics; mediating uses universal praise. Both include fiqh, but the second ingredient differs.
5
Note the unique terminology
These terms are not standard in Aristotelian logic. They show the author's adaptation of logic to Islamic governance.
Alert: shift from standard logic manuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[] وأما القياسات الحثية فهي التي تؤلف من مقدمات فقهية وسياسية فتؤخذ عاماتها من المقدمات الفقهية، وخاصاتها من المقدمات السياسية. [خ] وأما الوساطية فهي التي تؤلف من مقدمات مخلوطة مأخوذة من الفقهية والآراء المحمودة التي لا اختصاص لها بملة ملة نحو الحثة في العامة والخاصة.
[] As for hortatory syllogisms, they are composed of juridical and political premises: their generals are taken from juridical premises and their specifics from political premises. [Kh] As for mediating syllogisms, they are composed of mixed premises taken from juridical and praiseworthy opinions that are not specific to any religious community, like exhortation regarding the general and the specific.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage defines two additional types of syllogisms: hortatory (haththiyya) and mediating (wasatiyya). Hortatory syllogisms combine premises from juridical (fiqh) and political (siyasa) sources: the general premises come from fiqh, and the specific from politics. Mediating syllogisms use a mixture of juridical premises and praiseworthy opinions that are universal across communities, such as general exhortation applicable to both public and private matters. These categories show the author's detailed classification beyond the standard Aristotelian types.

The Three Main Syllogism Types: Theoretical, Deliberative, Rhetorical
Author differentiates theoretical, deliberative, and rhetorical syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between theoretical, deliberative, and rhetorical syllogisms based on premise truth and purpose.

Core Idea
Syllogisms differ: theoretical seeks truth, rhetorical seeks persuasion, deliberative seeks action.

This classification is crucial for understanding how Islamic scholars use logic differently in kalam (theoretical), fiqh (deliberative), and da'wa (rhetorical). The passage distinguishes these types by the nature of their premises and the intention of the speaker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of each syllogism type
Theoretical syllogisms aim at truth for oneself; deliberative aim at advising another on action; rhetorical aim at persuading an audience.
2
Note the requirement for premises
Theoretical syllogisms require true or mostly true premises in reality. Deliberative and rhetorical only require premises accepted and plausible to the listener.
3
Compare theoretical with political
Theoretical is more general than political: political concerns actions benefiting the city, while theoretical includes both that and other matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكر مع نفسه في إيثار ما يجب أن يفعل ولا يفعل أنه يخاطب. وكذلك يشبه أن المشورية تكون على الغير، ولذلك كانت القياسات التعقلية مأخوذة من مقدمات صادقة أو أكثرية في الحقيقة. وأما الخطبية والمشورية فليس الشرط فيها ذلك بل أن تكون مقبولة عند السامع مظنونة تلزمها النتيجة. وذلك لأن الإنسان ليس غرضه في نفسه أن يلزمها بل أن يهديها إلى الحق وغرضه مع غيره قد يكون الهداية وقد يكون الإلزام والتعقلية أعم من [ج] السياسية؛ إذ السياسية إنما تكون فيما ينتج ما ينتفع أن يفعل، وترك من الأمور المتعلقة بالمشاركة العائد نفعها إلى تدبير المدينة من حيث هو تدبيرها، والتعقلية تكون في ذلك وفيما هو الأخص من ذلك. [] وأما القياسات الحثية فهي التي تؤلف من مقدمات فقهية وسياسية فتؤخذ عاماتها من المقدمات الفقهية، وخاصاتها من المقدمات السياسية. [خ] وأما الوساطية فهي التي تؤلف من مقدمات مخلوطة مأخوذة من الفقهية والآراء المحمودة التي لا اختصاص لها بملة ملة نحو الحثة في العامة والخاصة.
He reflects with himself on preferring what ought to be done and what not to be done, that he addresses (another). And likewise it is similar that the deliberative is about the other. Therefore the theoretical syllogisms are taken from premises that are true or mostly true in reality. As for rhetorical and deliberative, the condition is not that, but rather that they be accepted by the listener, plausible, and that the result follows them. That is because man's purpose in himself is not that the result follows, but that it guides to the truth, and his purpose with others may be guidance or may be compulsion. And theoretical is more general than [j] the political; for political is only in what produces benefit to do and leave, from matters related to participation whose benefit returns to the administration of the city as such, whereas theoretical is in that and in what is more specific than that. [] As for causal syllogisms, they are composed from fiqh and political premises; their generals are taken from fiqh premises, and their specifics from political premises. [kh] As for intermediate syllogisms, they are composed from mixed premises taken from fiqh and praiseworthy opinions which are not specific to any religion, like the causal in general and specific.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of syllogistic reasoning types. The author begins by distinguishing between theoretical (تعقلية), deliberative (مشورية), and rhetorical (خطبية) syllogisms. Theoretical syllogisms aim at truth and use true or mostly true premises; deliberative and rhetorical syllogisms aim at persuasion and use premises that are accepted and plausible to the listener. The deliberative is about advising another on actions. Theoretical is broader than political (سياسية), which concerns actions that benefit the governance of the city. The author then introduces two additional types: causal (حثية) syllogisms, which combine fiqh and political premises (using fiqh for general premises and political for specifics), and intermediate (وساطية) syllogisms, which mix fiqh and praiseworthy opinions (آراء محمودة) not restricted to any one community. Thus the classification is based on the nature and source of premises and the purpose of the argument.

Key Terms
القياسات التعقلية
المشورية
الخطبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Syllogisms are classified by purpose and premise type: theoretical aims at truth with true premises; deliberative and rhetorical aim at persuasion with accepted premises.

Causal and Intermediate Syllogisms in Islamic Reasoning
Author defines causal and intermediate syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how causal (ḥaththiyya) and intermediate (wisāṭiyya) syllogisms combine fiqh and political premises.

Core Idea
Causal syllogisms blend fiqh general rules with political specifics; intermediate mix fiqh and universal praiseworthy opinions.

These less common types show how logic integrates legal and political reasoning in Islamic governance. The passage defines them as composed from mixed premises, offering a unique perspective on interdisciplinary reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of causal syllogisms
Causal syllogisms take their general premises from fiqh and their specific premises from political (siyāsa) considerations.
2
Identify the components of intermediate syllogisms
Intermediate syllogisms mix fiqh premises with praiseworthy opinions (ārāʾ maḥmūda) that are not specific to any religion.
3
Compare with earlier types
Unlike theoretical, these are applied types that deal with action; unlike rhetorical, they use composite premises from fiqh and political thought.

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of syllogistic reasoning types. The author begins by distinguishing between theoretical (تعقلية), deliberative (مشورية), and rhetorical (خطبية) syllogisms. Theoretical syllogisms aim at truth and use true or mostly true premises; deliberative and rhetorical syllogisms aim at persuasion and use premises that are accepted and plausible to the listener. The deliberative is about advising another on actions. Theoretical is broader than political (سياسية), which concerns actions that benefit the governance of the city. The author then introduces two additional types: causal (حثية) syllogisms, which combine fiqh and political premises (using fiqh for general premises and political for specifics), and intermediate (وساطية) syllogisms, which mix fiqh and praiseworthy opinions (آراء محمودة) not restricted to any one community. Thus the classification is based on the nature and source of premises and the purpose of the argument.

Key Terms
الحثية
الوساطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the source of the general premises in causal syllogisms?
A) Political premises
B) Fiqh premises
C) Rhetorical premises
D) Praiseworthy opinions

Key Takeaway
Causal and intermediate syllogisms demonstrate how fiqh and political considerations are combined in Islamic reasoning, using general fiqh rules and specific or universal non-religious premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a causal syllogism using a general fiqh principle (e.g., 'Intoxicants are forbidden') and a specific political premise (e.g., 'This substance is an intoxicant') to derive a ruling. Explain why it fits the definition.
?
Identify whether the following is causal or intermediate: 'All contracts should fulfill conditions (fiqh). This agreement meets the conditions (praiseworthy custom). Therefore this agreement is valid.' Justify your answer.

Induction as a Reduced Syllogism: Form and Epistemic Power
Author explains induction, its reduction to categorical syllogism, and its epistemological status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce an inductive argument to a categorical syllogism and assess its certainty based on the enumeration of particulars.

Core Idea
Induction is a syllogism with multiple middles; its certainty depends on exhaustive enumeration.

This passage from the logic text explains how induction (istiqra') is essentially a categorical syllogism in disguise, but with a crucial twist: the middle term is replaced by multiple instances. The author demonstrates this by an example about long-lived animals, and then clarifies the epistemic conditions under which induction yields certainty. Understanding this relationship is key to evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences that rely on inductive generalizations.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the inductive pattern
The passage states that in induction, we say 'every animal with little bile is either human, horse, or mule' and then 'every horse, mule, or human has little bile' to conclude 'every long-lived animal has little bile.' Identify the universal claim and the enumerated particulars.
The example uses three kinds of animals as instances.
2
Reduce to syllogistic form
The author says this reduces to categorical syllogism. Rewrite the argument as a standard syllogism: what would be the major premise, minor premise, and middle term? The middle term becomes the collection of individuals.
In the syllogism, the middle term would be 'being a human, horse, or mule'.
3
Analyze the epistemic condition
The passage distinguishes demonstrative induction (when particulars are finite and known) from non-demonstrative. Ask: When does induction give certainty? When does it only give conventional obligation?
The text says it may be demonstrative if the particulars are limited and known, yielding certainty.
4
Contrast with categorical syllogism
Note the structural difference: in syllogism, the minor term is the subject of the conclusion; in induction, what would be the minor term becomes a middle term. Also, obligation is conventional, not true.
The author says induction is for conventional obligation (ilzam mashhuri), not true obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا أردنا أن نستعمل هذا على سبيل الاستقراء قلنا الأوسط أصغر والأصغر أوسط؛ فقلنا كل حيوان قليل المرارة فهو إما إنسان أو فرس أو بغل، أو قلنا كل حيوان طويل العمر فهو كالإنسان والفرس والبغل. ثم قلنا وكل فرس أو بغل أو إنسان قليل المرارة فإنه ينتج أن كل حيوان طويل العمر فهو قليل المرارة. فقد رجع هذا إذن إلى القياس الاقتراني إلا أن الوسائط فيها أشياء كثيرة وهي موضوعات الحد الأكبر. فيجب أن تعلم أن الاستقراء ليس استقراء لأنه بيان حكم على كلي لكونه في جزئياته؛ وليس قانونه مبنيا على أن يكون حقا بل على أن يكون على الصفة التي مرَّ ذكرها؛ وإنه قد يكون برهانيًا كما إذا كانت الجزئيات محدودة معلومة يكون الحكم فيها على سبيل التيقن، وقد لا يكون كما إذا كان على خلاف هذا وإنه ليس للإلزام الحقيقي بل للإلزام المشهوري فيكون مخالفاً للقياس بهذه الجهة وبجهة أن الشيء الذي يجب أن يكون حدا أصغر إذا كان القول قياسا يصير في الاستقراء واسطة فيتبين ما يجب أن يكون حدا أكبر للواسطة. ثم القياس أقدم وأبين بالطبع عند العقل، والاستقراء أقدم وأبين عن الحس؛ وهذه كلها من جملة ما يعرف على سبيل التفصيل من بعد إن شاء الله تعالى.
If we want to use this by way of induction, we say: the middle term is the minor, and the minor is the middle; so we say: every animal with little bile is either a human, a horse, or a mule, or we say: every long-lived animal is like a human, a horse, and a mule. Then we say: and every horse, mule, or human has little bile, so it follows that every long-lived animal has little bile. Therefore, this reduces to the categorical syllogism, except that the middles are many things, which are the subjects of the major term. So you must know that induction is induction because it is a statement of a judgment about a universal due to its being in its particulars; and its rule is not based on being true but on being in the manner mentioned; and it may be demonstrative if the particulars are finite and known, the judgment being certain, and it may not be if the opposite; and it is not for true obligation but for conventional obligation, thus it differs from syllogism in this respect and in the respect that what should be the minor term in a syllogism becomes, in induction, a middle term through which what should be the major term for the middle is clarified. Then syllogism is prior and clearer by nature to the intellect, while induction is prior and clearer to the senses; and all these are among the matters that will be known in detail later, God willing.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage explains how induction (istiqra') relates to categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). The author begins by giving an example: to induce that long-lived animals have little bile, we use a middle term that is actually the minor term in a syllogism. The induction enumerates kinds of animals (human, horse, mule) and then states the rule, which reduces to a syllogism with multiple middles. The key point is that induction is not simply a statement of a universal from its particulars; its rule is not about absolute truth but about the enumerated instances. Induction can be demonstrative (burhani) if the particulars are finite and known, leading to certainty, or it can be non-demonstrative (dialectical) if not. Unlike syllogism which yields true obligation (ilzam haqiqi), induction yields conventional obligation (ilzam mashhuri). Structurally, what is the minor term in a syllogism becomes a middle term in induction. The author concludes by noting that syllogism is intellectually prior, while induction is sensorily prior.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
القياس الاقتراني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The number of particulars – finite and known vs. infinite
B) The type of conclusion – universal vs. particular
C) The subject matter – natural vs. supernatural
D) The syllogistic figure – first vs. second

Key Takeaway
Induction reduces to categorical syllogism but its certainty depends on exhaustive enumeration of particulars, and it yields conventional rather than true obligation.
