Why Two Affirmative Premises in the Second Figure Do Not Yield a Conclusion
Author analyzes the third method's syllogism and identifies its invalidity due to second-figure rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a second-figure categorical syllogism and explain why two affirmative premises do not produce a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
The second figure requires one negative premise; two affirmatives prove nothing.

In this passage, the author dissects a syllogism used by proponents of the third method. You will see how a syllogism with two affirmative premises in the second figure fails to yield a logical conclusion. Understanding this rule is essential for evaluating theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and figure
Find the two premises: 'the cause is an attribute' and 'the attribute is either B, J, or D'. Determine the figure by locating the middle term. Here the middle term 'attribute' is the predicate in both premises, making it the second figure (al-shakl al-thani).
2
Check the mood (premise types)
Both premises are affirmative. In categorical logic, the second figure requires one premise to be negative for a valid conclusion. Here both are affirmative, which is the invalid mood.
Rule: second figure needs one negative premise.
3
Apply the rules of the second figure
According to the rules of categorical syllogisms, the second figure only yields a conclusion if one premise is negative and the other is affirmative. Since both are affirmative, no conclusion follows necessarily.
4
See the consequence: the conclusion does not follow
The proponents incorrectly concluded that the cause is D. Because the syllogism is invalid, that conclusion is not logically forced. Hence the method is flawed for demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم في بيان كون الحدوث معللا بعلة فهو أن البناء حادث وحدوثه إما لذاته أو لعلة؛ وليس لذاته فهو لعلة. والعلة صفة، والصفة إما ب وإما ج وإما د، فالعلة إما ب وإما ج وإما د لكن ليس ب ولا ج فينتج أن العلة د لا أنها هي الدال؛ إذ موضوع النتيجة ومحمولها لا يعبر كل واحد منهما عما كانا عليه في المقدمتين. ولا يمكنهم أن يقولوا والعلة صفة والصفة إما هي الباء أو الجيم أو الدال حتى تكون النتيجة أن هذه العلة هي الدال، ولا أن يقولوا إما أن يكون ب صفة أو ج صفة أو د صفة لكذب القضيتين أولا، فإن كل ذلك صفة لا واحد منها فقط. ولعدم إنتاج الوضع الثاني فإنهما موجبتان من الشكل الثاني. وإذا كانت النتيجة إن العلة هي كذا فهذه قضية عامتها أن تصير كلية فيكون كل ما هو علة للحدوث جسما والقضية الكلية لا تنعكس فلا تصير كل ما هو جسم علة للحدوث هذا هو بيان فاسد ما يكون أقوى الطرق عندهم إلا أنه. وإن كان كذلك فلا بأس بأن يستعمل في الجدل؛ إذ ليس المطلوب فيه التيقن بل إقناع النفس وتطييبه بما يعتقد في المشهور أنه منتج، هذا إذا كان المطلوب كليا. فأما إذا كان جزئياً وأريد إثباته بالمثال فالمثال في إثباته قياس منتج من الشكل الثالث كما إذا قلت البناء جسم والبناء محدث ينتج أن بعض الجسم محدث. ولكن لا يلزم أن ذلك الجسم هو الجسم المنازع فيه وهو السماء أو غيره من الأمور المنازع فيها. وقد نقل عن الشيخ في ضعف هذا الطريق وجوه أولها أن يقال لا نسلم أن الحدوث معلل بالعلة بل لا يكون فإنه إذا كان من اللوازم أن يكون كل حكم معللا بالعلة لكان من اللوازم أن تكون علة ذلك الحكم، وعليتها أيضًا معللًا بعلة أخرى إلى غير النهاية؛ وذلك محال. وإن سلمنا أنه معلل لكن لم لا
And as for their statement in explaining that temporality is caused by a cause, it is that the building is originated, and its temporality is either due to itself or due to a cause; and it is not due to itself, so it is due to a cause. And the cause is an attribute, and the attribute is either B or J or D, so the cause is either B or J or D. But it is not B nor J, so it results that the cause is D, not that it is the sign (al-dal); for the subject and predicate of the conclusion do not each express what they were in the two premises. And they cannot say: the cause is an attribute, and the attribute is either B or J or D, so that the conclusion would be that this cause is D, nor can they say: either B is an attribute or J is an attribute or D is an attribute, because the two propositions would be false, for all of that is an attribute, not one of them alone. And due to the non-production of the second position, for they are two affirmative [propositions] in the second figure. And if the conclusion is 'the cause is such-and-such,' then this proposition generally becomes universal, so that everything that is a cause of temporality is a body, and the universal proposition does not convert, so it does not become 'everything that is a body is a cause of temporality.' This is the exposition of the corruption of what is considered the strongest method among them. However, if it is so, there is no harm in using it in dialectic, for what is required there is not certainty but persuasion of the soul and making it content with what is believed in the common opinion to be productive. This if the sought is universal. As for if it is particular and one wants to prove it by example, then the example in proving it is a productive syllogism from the third figure, as when you say: the building is a body, and the building is originated, it results that some body is originated. But it does not follow that that body is the body in dispute, which is the sky or other things in dispute. And it has been transmitted from the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] concerning the weakness of this method, several points. The first is to say: we do not grant that temporality is caused by a cause; rather, it is not, for if it were necessary that every judgment is caused by a cause, then it would be necessary that the cause of that judgment is also caused by another cause, ad infinitum; and that is impossible. And if we grant that it is caused, then why not...
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage dissects the third method's underlying reasoning. The proponents argue: the building is originated; its temporality is either due to itself or a cause; not due to itself, so due to a cause. The cause is an attribute, and attributes are only B, J, or D (placeholder variables). Since it is not B or J, it must be D, where D stands for 'body' (jism). But the author points out a logical flaw: the conclusion that the cause is D does not follow simply because the cause is an attribute; the syllogism as stated is in the second figure (two affirmative premises) which does not yield a valid conclusion. Moreover, even if the conclusion were universal—'every cause of temporality is a body'—it does not convert to 'every body is a cause of temporality,' which is what the proponents actually need. Thus the method is invalid for demonstration. However, the author notes it can still be used in dialectic because dialectic aims at persuasion, not certainty. If the sought is particular, one could use a third-figure syllogism (e.g., 'the building is a body and originated, therefore some body is originated'), but that does not identify the specific body in dispute (e.g., the sky). Finally, the author quotes a critique attributed to the Shaykh (Ibn Sina): the very assumption that every judgment must have a cause leads to an infinite regress; thus it is not granted that temporality is caused.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني
الشكل الثالث
الانعكاس
الجدل
الشيخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the second figure with two affirmative premises not yield a valid conclusion?
A) Because the middle term is the subject in both premises.
B) Because one premise must be negative in the second figure.
C) Because the conclusion must be particular.
D) Because the premises are not universal.

Key Takeaway
The second figure of the categorical syllogism requires at least one negative premise; two affirmatives produce no necessary conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following syllogism: 'Every created thing is contingent. Every contingent thing is created. Therefore, every created thing is contingent.' Identify the figure and determine if it is valid.

Why Universal Affirmative Does Not Convert to Universal Affirmative
Author explains why the universal conclusion does not convert as needed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform conversion of categorical propositions and identify invalid conversion attempts.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative (A) converts to a particular affirmative (I), not to another A.

The author notes that even if the conclusion 'every cause of temporality is a body' were true, it does not convert to 'every body is a cause of temporality'. Understanding conversion rules is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition to be converted
The text states: 'If the conclusion is 'the cause is such-and-such,' then this proposition generally becomes universal: 'every cause of temporality is a body'. This is a universal affirmative (A) proposition.
2
Recall the conversion rule for universal affirmative
In categorical logic, a universal affirmative (All S are P) converts to a particular affirmative (Some P are S). It does not convert to a universal affirmative (All P are S).
3
Apply the rule to this case
The proponents need the conclusion 'every body is a cause of temporality' to identify the specific body (e.g., the sky). But that would be an invalid conversion from 'every cause of temporality is a body' because the predicate becomes subject and the quantity changes.
4
See why this invalidates the method
Since the required conversion is invalid, the method cannot prove that a specific body (like the sky) is the cause. Thus the method fails for its intended purpose.

The Syllogism
intermediate

When a Flawed Syllogism Can Still Be Used in Dialectic
Author explains that the flawed syllogism can still be used in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between demonstration (burhan) and dialectic (jadal) based on their epistemic aims.

Core Idea
Dialectic aims at persuasion, not certainty; a flawed argument can still persuade the common person.

The author notes that despite its logical flaws, the third method can still be used in dialectic (jadal) because the goal there is not certainty but persuasion. Understanding this distinction is key to evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and debate.

Guided Path
1
Define demonstration and dialectic
Demonstration (burhan) aims at certainty (yaqin) using true premises. Dialectic (jadal) aims at persuasion (iqna') using premises that are commonly accepted (mashhurat).
2
Identify the author's statement
The author says: 'there is no harm in using it in dialectic, for what is required there is not certainty but persuasion.'
3
Understand why a flawed argument can be persuasive
In dialectic, the goal is to convince the opponent or audience using what they believe. Even if the argument is logically invalid, if it appears valid to common opinion, it can be persuasive.
4
Recognize the limitation
The same argument cannot be used for demonstration because it does not produce certainty. This shows that the method is only acceptable in a dialectical context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم في بيان كون الحدوث معللا بعلة فهو أن البناء حادث وحدوثه إما لذاته أو لعلة؛ وليس لذاته فهو لعلة. والعلة صفة، والصفة إما ب وإما ج وإما د، فالعلة إما ب وإما ج وإما د لكن ليس ب ولا ج فينتج أن العلة د لا أنها هي الدال؛ إذ موضوع النتيجة ومحمولها لا يعبر كل واحد منهما عما كانا عليه في المقدمتين. ولا يمكنهم أن يقولوا والعلة صفة والصفة إما هي الباء أو الجيم أو الدال حتى تكون النتيجة أن هذه العلة هي الدال، ولا أن يقولوا إما أن يكون ب صفة أو ج صفة أو د صفة لكذب القضيتين أولا، فإن كل ذلك صفة لا واحد منها فقط. ولعدم إنتاج الوضع الثاني فإنهما موجبتان من الشكل الثاني. وإذا كانت النتيجة إن العلة هي كذا فهذه قضية عامتها أن تصير كلية فيكون كل ما هو علة للحدوث جسما والقضية الكلية لا تنعكس فلا تصير كل ما هو جسم علة للحدوث هذا هو بيان فاسد ما يكون أقوى الطرق عندهم إلا أنه. وإن كان كذلك فلا بأس بأن يستعمل في الجدل؛ إذ ليس المطلوب فيه التيقن بل إقناع النفس وتطييبه بما يعتقد في المشهور أنه منتج، هذا إذا كان المطلوب كليا. فأما إذا كان جزئياً وأريد إثباته بالمثال فالمثال في إثباته قياس منتج من الشكل الثالث كما إذا قلت البناء جسم والبناء محدث ينتج أن بعض الجسم محدث. ولكن لا يلزم أن ذلك الجسم هو الجسم المنازع فيه وهو السماء أو غيره من الأمور المنازع فيها. وقد نقل عن الشيخ في ضعف هذا الطريق وجوه أولها أن يقال لا نسلم أن الحدوث معلل بالعلة بل لا يكون فإنه إذا كان من اللوازم أن يكون كل حكم معللا بالعلة لكان من اللوازم أن تكون علة ذلك الحكم، وعليتها أيضًا معللًا بعلة أخرى إلى غير النهاية؛ وذلك محال. وإن سلمنا أنه معلل لكن لم لا
And as for their statement in explaining that temporality is caused by a cause, it is that the building is originated, and its temporality is either due to itself or due to a cause; and it is not due to itself, so it is due to a cause. And the cause is an attribute, and the attribute is either B or J or D, so the cause is either B or J or D. But it is not B nor J, so it results that the cause is D, not that it is the sign (al-dal); for the subject and predicate of the conclusion do not each express what they were in the two premises. And they cannot say: the cause is an attribute, and the attribute is either B or J or D, so that the conclusion would be that this cause is D, nor can they say: either B is an attribute or J is an attribute or D is an attribute, because the two propositions would be false, for all of that is an attribute, not one of them alone. And due to the non-production of the second position, for they are two affirmative [propositions] in the second figure. And if the conclusion is 'the cause is such-and-such,' then this proposition generally becomes universal, so that everything that is a cause of temporality is a body, and the universal proposition does not convert, so it does not become 'everything that is a body is a cause of temporality.' This is the exposition of the corruption of what is considered the strongest method among them. However, if it is so, there is no harm in using it in dialectic, for what is required there is not certainty but persuasion of the soul and making it content with what is believed in the common opinion to be productive. This if the sought is universal. As for if it is particular and one wants to prove it by example, then the example in proving it is a productive syllogism from the third figure, as when you say: the building is a body, and the building is originated, it results that some body is originated. But it does not follow that that body is the body in dispute, which is the sky or other things in dispute. And it has been transmitted from the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] concerning the weakness of this method, several points. The first is to say: we do not grant that temporality is caused by a cause; rather, it is not, for if it were necessary that every judgment is caused by a cause, then it would be necessary that the cause of that judgment is also caused by another cause, ad infinitum; and that is impossible. And if we grant that it is caused, then why not...
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage dissects the third method's underlying reasoning. The proponents argue: the building is originated; its temporality is either due to itself or a cause; not due to itself, so due to a cause. The cause is an attribute, and attributes are only B, J, or D (placeholder variables). Since it is not B or J, it must be D, where D stands for 'body' (jism). But the author points out a logical flaw: the conclusion that the cause is D does not follow simply because the cause is an attribute; the syllogism as stated is in the second figure (two affirmative premises) which does not yield a valid conclusion. Moreover, even if the conclusion were universal—'every cause of temporality is a body'—it does not convert to 'every body is a cause of temporality,' which is what the proponents actually need. Thus the method is invalid for demonstration. However, the author notes it can still be used in dialectic because dialectic aims at persuasion, not certainty. If the sought is particular, one could use a third-figure syllogism (e.g., 'the building is a body and originated, therefore some body is originated'), but that does not identify the specific body in dispute (e.g., the sky). Finally, the author quotes a critique attributed to the Shaykh (Ibn Sina): the very assumption that every judgment must have a cause leads to an infinite regress; thus it is not granted that temporality is caused.

Key Terms
الجدل
البرهان
الإقناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the third method be used in dialectic?
A) Because it is a valid demonstration.
B) Because dialectic aims at persuasion, not certainty.
C) Because the premises are true.
D) Because it is from the third figure.

Key Takeaway
Dialectic accepts arguments that are persuasive in common opinion, even if they are not logically valid for demonstration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an example in kalam literature where a syllogism that is logically invalid is still used to persuade. Explain why it works in a dialectical context.

Ibn Sina's Critique: The Infinite Regress of Causes
Author quotes Ibn Sina's first objection: the assumption of causation leads to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Sina's infinite regress argument against the claim that every judgment has a cause.

Core Idea
If every judgment must have a cause, that cause itself must have a cause, leading to an impossible infinite regress.

The author quotes Ibn Sina's first objection: the very assumption that temporality is caused by a cause is problematic. If every judgment needs a cause, then the cause itself would need a cause, and so on ad infinitum, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assumption under attack
The proponents assume that temporality (huduth) is caused by a cause (illah). This is the principle that every originated thing has a cause.
2
State Ibn Sina's infinite regress argument
If every judgment has a cause, then the judgment that 'the cause is the cause of temporality' also has a cause. That cause would also need a cause, leading to an infinite chain (tasalsul).
3
Explain why the regress is impossible
An infinite regress of causes is impossible in the Aristotelian/Islamic framework because it would require an actual infinity, which is considered impossible (muhal).
4
See how this undermines the third method
By rejecting the root assumption, Ibn Sina removes the foundation of the third method's syllogism. Even if the syllogism were valid, the premise that temporality is caused is not granted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Definition and Purpose of Muqawamah
Author defines muqawamah and outlines its purpose and types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition, purpose, and two types of the syllogism of resistance (muqawamah).

Core Idea
Muqawamah is a counter-syllogism designed to invalidate a premise of another syllogism.

In Islamic dialectic, when a debater seeks to refute an opponent's syllogism, they often construct a counter-syllogism that produces the opposite of one of the opponent's premises. This tool is called muqawamah. The author defines it and explains its purpose: to attack the universal premise, which is the most evident part of the opponent's argument. He then introduces the two methods: inad and munaqadah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined – 'qiyas al-muqawamah'.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
Genus: 'a syllogism composed of two premises'. Differentia: 'producing a conclusion opposite to a premise in another syllogism'.
3
Determine the purpose
Why is muqawamah used? To invalidate a premise, specifically the universal premise of the original syllogism.
4
Note the two types
The author mentions two ways: inad and munaqadah. This sets up the subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في قياس المقاومة] وأما قياس المقاومة فهو قياس مؤلف من مقدمتين منتج مقابل مقدمة هي في الغير من الأقيسة. ولما كان منتجًا مقابل مقدمة من مقدمات ذلك القياس كان مبطلا ذلك فالمقاومة يقصد بها قصد إبطال المقدّمة الكلية التي في القياس فإنها أبين القياس؛ وذلك على وجهين : إما عنادًا وإما مناقضة. [أ] أما العناد فهو أن تجعل المقدّمة الكبرى في القياس الذي تقابل به المقدمة الكلية أشد عموماً من تلك المقدمة ومخالفة لها في الكيفية مثلا إذا كان الحكم في المقدّمة الأولى على شيء عام كالأضداد، ويكون الحكم عليها أن العلم بها واحد فنجيء المقاوم ونأخذ ما هو أعم من الأضداد ونحكم عليه بضد الحكم الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو أن يسلب عنه ذلك الحكم سلبًا كليا فنقول ولا شيء من المتقابلات يكون العلم بها واحدًا، ونضيف إليه قولنا إن المتضادات متقابلات لينتج مقابل المقدّمة المذكورة. ثمَّ هذا القياس إذا كان منتجا ما يقابل كبرى القياس الأول وتضاف إليه الصغرى من القياس الأول ينتج مقابل النتيجة المطلوبة بالقياس الأول مثلا القياس الأول قولنا «بج» وكل «ج أ» ينتجان ب أ)، فعُورض أن «ب» ليس «أ» لأن (ب) ج» ولا شيء من ج أ» ينتجان «ب» ليس الذي هو مقابل نتيجة القياس الأول. [ب] وأما المناقضة فهي أن تكون الدعوى في القياس الأول كليًّا فيورد المقاوم جزئيا من الموضوع ليس فيه الحكم المذكور، ويكون ذلك الجزئي ملحوظا إليه أول ما يلحظ للمناقضة على أنه موضوع لطرفي المناقضة؛ وهذه الهيئة ليست هيئة الشكل الأول ولا الشكل الثاني. فإذن كل مقاومة موجبة؛ فإما في الشكل الأول، وإما في الشكل الثالث. وأما في الشكل الثاني فلا يمكن مقاومة موجبة. وأما السالبة فلا تبين به إلا وأن يعبر الكلام في الكبرى عن وجه المقاومة وهو أن يكون موضوعه هو بعينه موضوع المقاومة مثلا نقول إن الأضداد متقابلة ولا شيء مما به العلم واحد بمقابل فيعتبر الدلالة على البيان بنفسه إلى إثباته بالعكس فإن البين بنفسه إذا كان أنه لا شيء من المتقابلات العلم به واحدًا فيؤخذ بدل هذا عكسه لم يذعن له الذهن إذعانه لهذا بل احتاج أن يذكر أن السالبة نتعكس فيحتاج أن يريد في الكلام ما عنه غنى ونحن وإن قلنا إن السالبة الكلية تنعكس مثل نفسها فلم نضمن أن عكسها مثلها في البيان. ويجب أن نتأمل في المقاومة ما يوجبه الرأي المحمود فتؤخذ المقاومة مقدمة
Section: On the Syllogism of Resistance. As for the syllogism of resistance, it is a syllogism composed of two premises producing a conclusion opposite to a premise that is in another syllogism. Since it produces an opposite to a premise of that syllogism, it invalidates it. So the purpose of resistance is to invalidate the universal premise in the syllogism, for it is the most evident part of the syllogism. And that is in two ways: either by obstinacy (inad) or by contradiction (munaqadah). [A] As for obstinacy, it is that you make the major premise in the syllogism with which you oppose the universal premise more general in scope than that premise and opposite in quality. For example, if the ruling in the first premise is about something general like 'opposites', and the ruling on it is that knowledge of them is one, we come as the resistor and take what is more general than 'opposites' and rule on it with the opposite of that ruling, namely we deny that ruling universally, saying 'No correlatives have knowledge of them as one', and we add our statement 'Opposites are correlatives', to produce the opposite of the mentioned premise. Then this syllogism, if it produces the opposite of the major of the first syllogism and the minor of the first syllogism is added to it, it produces the opposite of the desired conclusion of the first syllogism. For example, the first syllogism is our statement 'B is J' and 'Every J is A' producing 'B is A'. It is objected that 'B is not A', because 'B is J' and 'No J is A' produce 'B is not A', which is the opposite of the conclusion of the first syllogism. [B] As for contradiction, it is that the claim in the first syllogism is universal, so the resistor brings a particular of the subject that does not have the mentioned ruling, and that particular is noted first when looking for contradiction, as the subject for the two terms of contradiction. This form is not the form of the first figure nor the second figure. So every positive resistance is either in the first figure or the third figure. As for the second figure, a positive resistance is impossible. As for the negative, it is not evident except that the speech in the major indicates the aspect of resistance, namely that its subject is exactly the subject of the resistance. For example, we say 'Opposites are correlatives, and nothing that has one knowledge is a correlative'—then the indication of self-evidence is considered to prove it by conversion. For if it is self-evident that no correlatives have one knowledge, and we take its converse instead, the mind would not assent to it as it does to this, but would need to mention that the negative converts—so the speech becomes redundant. And even if we say that the universal negative converts as itself, we do not guarantee that its converse is equal to it in evidence. And one must reflect on resistance as sound opinion requires, so that the resistance is taken as a premise.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the 'syllogism of resistance' (qiyas al-muqawamah), a dialectical tool used to refute a premise of another syllogism. The author begins by stating that muqawamah is a syllogism of two premises that produces a conclusion opposite to a premise in another syllogism, thereby invalidating it. Its purpose is to attack the universal premise (often the major) of the original syllogism, which is the most evident part. There are two types: inad (obstinacy) and munaqadah (contradiction). In inad, the resistor selects a term more general than the subject of the target premise and makes a statement of opposite quality (affirmative vs. negative). An example is given: the original premise 'Every opposite has one knowledge' (or more precisely, the judgment that knowledge of opposites is one); the resistor takes 'correlatives' (more general than 'opposites') and says 'No correlative has one knowledge', then adds 'Every opposite is a correlative' to conclude the opposite of the original premise. This counter-syllogism, when combined with the minor of the original, yields a conclusion opposite to the original conclusion. The author then illustrates with symbolic letters: the original 'B is J, every J is A, therefore B is A' is opposed by 'B is J, no J is A, therefore B is not A'. In munaqadah, the resistor presents a particular instance of the subject that does not bear the universal judgment. The form of this resistance is not that of the first or second figure; the author later clarifies that positive muqawamah occurs only in the first or third figures, never in the second. The passage then discusses negative muqawamah, noting that it is not self-evident unless the major's subject is exactly the subject of the resistance. An example is given: 'Opposites are correlatives; nothing that has one knowledge is a correlative'—but this conclusion is not immediate; one might think to convert the negative premise, but the author warns that conversion of a negative does not carry the same evidence. The passage ends by urging careful reflection to follow sound opinion when constructing a muqawamah. This is a detailed expansion on a dialectical concept, typical of a commentary (sharh) that probes and justifies the matn claim.

Key Terms
قياس المقاومة
عناد
مناقضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the purpose of muqawamah?
A) To prove a new conclusion.
B) To invalidate a universal premise in another syllogism.
C) To strengthen the original syllogism.
D) To convert a negative proposition.

Key Takeaway
Muqawamah is a counter-syllogism that produces a conclusion opposite to a premise of another syllogism, aiming to invalidate that premise, especially the universal one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a muqawamah against the universal premise 'Every act is either obligatory or forbidden' by using the inad method. Take a more general term and opposite quality.
?
Identify which type of muqawamah (inad or munaqadah) would be appropriate to refute the claim 'All intoxicants are forbidden' if you want to present a specific intoxicant that is not forbidden.

advanced
8 min
Inad (Obstinacy): Construction and Example
Author explains inad with an example and symbolic representation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct an inad counter-syllogism by selecting a more general term and opposite quality, and verify its logical validity.

Core Idea
Inad uses a more general term with opposite quality to flip a universal premise.

In the first type of muqawamah, called inad, the resistor attacks a universal premise by selecting a term that is more general in scope than the subject of the premise and making a statement of opposite quality (affirmative vs. negative). The author provides a concrete example using 'opposites' and 'correlatives', and illustrates with symbolic letters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target premise
In the example, the target is 'Every opposite has one knowledge' (affirmative universal).
2
Find a more general term
The resistor takes 'correlatives' which is more general than 'opposites' (since opposites are a type of correlatives).
3
Determine the opposite quality
Since the target is affirmative, the counter-premise is negative: 'No correlatives have one knowledge'.
4
Add a linking premise
Add 'Every opposite is a correlative' to connect the terms.
5
Check the figure and validity
This is a first figure syllogism (Celarent). The conclusion 'No opposites have one knowledge' is the opposite of the original premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في قياس المقاومة] وأما قياس المقاومة فهو قياس مؤلف من مقدمتين منتج مقابل مقدمة هي في الغير من الأقيسة. ولما كان منتجًا مقابل مقدمة من مقدمات ذلك القياس كان مبطلا ذلك فالمقاومة يقصد بها قصد إبطال المقدّمة الكلية التي في القياس فإنها أبين القياس؛ وذلك على وجهين : إما عنادًا وإما مناقضة. [أ] أما العناد فهو أن تجعل المقدّمة الكبرى في القياس الذي تقابل به المقدمة الكلية أشد عموماً من تلك المقدمة ومخالفة لها في الكيفية مثلا إذا كان الحكم في المقدّمة الأولى على شيء عام كالأضداد، ويكون الحكم عليها أن العلم بها واحد فنجيء المقاوم ونأخذ ما هو أعم من الأضداد ونحكم عليه بضد الحكم الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو أن يسلب عنه ذلك الحكم سلبًا كليا فنقول ولا شيء من المتقابلات يكون العلم بها واحدًا، ونضيف إليه قولنا إن المتضادات متقابلات لينتج مقابل المقدّمة المذكورة. ثمَّ هذا القياس إذا كان منتجا ما يقابل كبرى القياس الأول وتضاف إليه الصغرى من القياس الأول ينتج مقابل النتيجة المطلوبة بالقياس الأول مثلا القياس الأول قولنا «بج» وكل «ج أ» ينتجان ب أ)، فعُورض أن «ب» ليس «أ» لأن (ب) ج» ولا شيء من ج أ» ينتجان «ب» ليس الذي هو مقابل نتيجة القياس الأول. [ب] وأما المناقضة فهي أن تكون الدعوى في القياس الأول كليًّا فيورد المقاوم جزئيا من الموضوع ليس فيه الحكم المذكور، ويكون ذلك الجزئي ملحوظا إليه أول ما يلحظ للمناقضة على أنه موضوع لطرفي المناقضة؛ وهذه الهيئة ليست هيئة الشكل الأول ولا الشكل الثاني. فإذن كل مقاومة موجبة؛ فإما في الشكل الأول، وإما في الشكل الثالث. وأما في الشكل الثاني فلا يمكن مقاومة موجبة. وأما السالبة فلا تبين به إلا وأن يعبر الكلام في الكبرى عن وجه المقاومة وهو أن يكون موضوعه هو بعينه موضوع المقاومة مثلا نقول إن الأضداد متقابلة ولا شيء مما به العلم واحد بمقابل فيعتبر الدلالة على البيان بنفسه إلى إثباته بالعكس فإن البين بنفسه إذا كان أنه لا شيء من المتقابلات العلم به واحدًا فيؤخذ بدل هذا عكسه لم يذعن له الذهن إذعانه لهذا بل احتاج أن يذكر أن السالبة نتعكس فيحتاج أن يريد في الكلام ما عنه غنى ونحن وإن قلنا إن السالبة الكلية تنعكس مثل نفسها فلم نضمن أن عكسها مثلها في البيان. ويجب أن نتأمل في المقاومة ما يوجبه الرأي المحمود فتؤخذ المقاومة مقدمة
Section: On the Syllogism of Resistance. As for the syllogism of resistance, it is a syllogism composed of two premises producing a conclusion opposite to a premise that is in another syllogism. Since it produces an opposite to a premise of that syllogism, it invalidates it. So the purpose of resistance is to invalidate the universal premise in the syllogism, for it is the most evident part of the syllogism. And that is in two ways: either by obstinacy (inad) or by contradiction (munaqadah). [A] As for obstinacy, it is that you make the major premise in the syllogism with which you oppose the universal premise more general in scope than that premise and opposite in quality. For example, if the ruling in the first premise is about something general like 'opposites', and the ruling on it is that knowledge of them is one, we come as the resistor and take what is more general than 'opposites' and rule on it with the opposite of that ruling, namely we deny that ruling universally, saying 'No correlatives have knowledge of them as one', and we add our statement 'Opposites are correlatives', to produce the opposite of the mentioned premise. Then this syllogism, if it produces the opposite of the major of the first syllogism and the minor of the first syllogism is added to it, it produces the opposite of the desired conclusion of the first syllogism. For example, the first syllogism is our statement 'B is J' and 'Every J is A' producing 'B is A'. It is objected that 'B is not A', because 'B is J' and 'No J is A' produce 'B is not A', which is the opposite of the conclusion of the first syllogism. [B] As for contradiction, it is that the claim in the first syllogism is universal, so the resistor brings a particular of the subject that does not have the mentioned ruling, and that particular is noted first when looking for contradiction, as the subject for the two terms of contradiction. This form is not the form of the first figure nor the second figure. So every positive resistance is either in the first figure or the third figure. As for the second figure, a positive resistance is impossible. As for the negative, it is not evident except that the speech in the major indicates the aspect of resistance, namely that its subject is exactly the subject of the resistance. For example, we say 'Opposites are correlatives, and nothing that has one knowledge is a correlative'—then the indication of self-evidence is considered to prove it by conversion. For if it is self-evident that no correlatives have one knowledge, and we take its converse instead, the mind would not assent to it as it does to this, but would need to mention that the negative converts—so the speech becomes redundant. And even if we say that the universal negative converts as itself, we do not guarantee that its converse is equal to it in evidence. And one must reflect on resistance as sound opinion requires, so that the resistance is taken as a premise.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the 'syllogism of resistance' (qiyas al-muqawamah), a dialectical tool used to refute a premise of another syllogism. The author begins by stating that muqawamah is a syllogism of two premises that produces a conclusion opposite to a premise in another syllogism, thereby invalidating it. Its purpose is to attack the universal premise (often the major) of the original syllogism, which is the most evident part. There are two types: inad (obstinacy) and munaqadah (contradiction). In inad, the resistor selects a term more general than the subject of the target premise and makes a statement of opposite quality (affirmative vs. negative). An example is given: the original premise 'Every opposite has one knowledge' (or more precisely, the judgment that knowledge of opposites is one); the resistor takes 'correlatives' (more general than 'opposites') and says 'No correlative has one knowledge', then adds 'Every opposite is a correlative' to conclude the opposite of the original premise. This counter-syllogism, when combined with the minor of the original, yields a conclusion opposite to the original conclusion. The author then illustrates with symbolic letters: the original 'B is J, every J is A, therefore B is A' is opposed by 'B is J, no J is A, therefore B is not A'. In munaqadah, the resistor presents a particular instance of the subject that does not bear the universal judgment. The form of this resistance is not that of the first or second figure; the author later clarifies that positive muqawamah occurs only in the first or third figures, never in the second. The passage then discusses negative muqawamah, noting that it is not self-evident unless the major's subject is exactly the subject of the resistance. An example is given: 'Opposites are correlatives; nothing that has one knowledge is a correlative'—but this conclusion is not immediate; one might think to convert the negative premise, but the author warns that conversion of a negative does not carry the same evidence. The passage ends by urging careful reflection to follow sound opinion when constructing a muqawamah. This is a detailed expansion on a dialectical concept, typical of a commentary (sharh) that probes and justifies the matn claim.

Key Terms
عناد
قياس المقاومة
الأضداد
المتقابلات

Checkpoint
In the example of inad, what is the more general term used by the resistor?
A) الأضداد (opposites)
B) المتقابلات (correlatives)
C) العلم (knowledge)
D) الكلية (universal)

Key Takeaway
Inad constructs a counter-syllogism with a more general term and opposite quality, typically in first figure, to produce the opposite of the target premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an inad counter-syllogism against the universal premise 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. Select a more general term (e.g., 'every beverage') and opposite quality (negative). Ensure the syllogism is in first figure and valid.
?
Check the validity of the following inad: Target: 'All knowledge is beneficial.' Counter: 'All thinking is not beneficial' and 'All knowledge is thinking'. Is this a valid first figure? Does it produce the opposite conclusion?

Munaqadah and Figures of Positive Muqawamah
Author defines munaqadah and discusses permissible figures for positive muqawamah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish munaqadah from inad and identify which figures allow positive muqawamah.

Core Idea
Munaqadah uses a particular instance to contradict a universal claim, and positive muqawamah only works in first and third figures.

The second type of muqawamah is munaqadah or contradiction. Here, the resistor does not take a more general term but instead brings a specific instance of the subject that does not have the universal ruling. The author also clarifies a crucial point about figures: positive muqawamah (one that yields an affirmative conclusion) can only occur in the first or third figure, never in the second. This lesson unpacks these concepts.

Guided Path
1
Understand munaqadah
The resistor looks for a particular (juz'i) of the subject that lacks the predicate claimed universally.
2
Note the form
This is not a syllogism in first or second figure; the author does not specify the figure but implies it is a different structure.
3
Analyze the figure limitation for positive muqawamah
The author states that every positive muqawamah is either in first or third figure.
4
Consider why second figure is impossible
In second figure, the conclusion is always negative, so a positive conclusion (affirmative) cannot be produced.

Negative Muqawamah and the Role of Conversion
Author discusses pitfalls of negative muqawamah and conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the challenges of negative muqawamah and explain why conversion does not preserve self-evidence.

Core Idea
Negative muqawamah is less evident and cannot rely on simple conversion to establish its force.

The author concludes the section with a discussion of negative muqawamah (one that yields a negative conclusion). He points out that it is not self-evident unless the subject in the major is identical to the subject of the resistance. He also warns against using conversion of a negative proposition as a shortcut, because the converted form does not carry the same evidentiary weight. This lesson explores the subtlety of negative muqawamah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Negative muqawamah aims to produce a negative conclusion opposite to a premise.
2
Note the condition for self-evidence
For it to be evident, the subject of the major premise must be exactly the subject of the resistance.
3
Analyze the example
'Opposites are correlatives; nothing that has one knowledge is a correlative' – this yields the desired negative but requires that the first premise be converted or understood differently.
4
Understand the conversion trap
The author explains that if we take the converse of a self-evident negative, it loses its self-evidence because conversion changes the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في قياس المقاومة] وأما قياس المقاومة فهو قياس مؤلف من مقدمتين منتج مقابل مقدمة هي في الغير من الأقيسة. ولما كان منتجًا مقابل مقدمة من مقدمات ذلك القياس كان مبطلا ذلك فالمقاومة يقصد بها قصد إبطال المقدّمة الكلية التي في القياس فإنها أبين القياس؛ وذلك على وجهين : إما عنادًا وإما مناقضة. [أ] أما العناد فهو أن تجعل المقدّمة الكبرى في القياس الذي تقابل به المقدمة الكلية أشد عموماً من تلك المقدمة ومخالفة لها في الكيفية مثلا إذا كان الحكم في المقدّمة الأولى على شيء عام كالأضداد، ويكون الحكم عليها أن العلم بها واحد فنجيء المقاوم ونأخذ ما هو أعم من الأضداد ونحكم عليه بضد الحكم الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو أن يسلب عنه ذلك الحكم سلبًا كليا فنقول ولا شيء من المتقابلات يكون العلم بها واحدًا، ونضيف إليه قولنا إن المتضادات متقابلات لينتج مقابل المقدّمة المذكورة. ثمَّ هذا القياس إذا كان منتجا ما يقابل كبرى القياس الأول وتضاف إليه الصغرى من القياس الأول ينتج مقابل النتيجة المطلوبة بالقياس الأول مثلا القياس الأول قولنا «بج» وكل «ج أ» ينتجان ب أ)، فعُورض أن «ب» ليس «أ» لأن (ب) ج» ولا شيء من ج أ» ينتجان «ب» ليس الذي هو مقابل نتيجة القياس الأول. [ب] وأما المناقضة فهي أن تكون الدعوى في القياس الأول كليًّا فيورد المقاوم جزئيا من الموضوع ليس فيه الحكم المذكور، ويكون ذلك الجزئي ملحوظا إليه أول ما يلحظ للمناقضة على أنه موضوع لطرفي المناقضة؛ وهذه الهيئة ليست هيئة الشكل الأول ولا الشكل الثاني. فإذن كل مقاومة موجبة؛ فإما في الشكل الأول، وإما في الشكل الثالث. وأما في الشكل الثاني فلا يمكن مقاومة موجبة. وأما السالبة فلا تبين به إلا وأن يعبر الكلام في الكبرى عن وجه المقاومة وهو أن يكون موضوعه هو بعينه موضوع المقاومة مثلا نقول إن الأضداد متقابلة ولا شيء مما به العلم واحد بمقابل فيعتبر الدلالة على البيان بنفسه إلى إثباته بالعكس فإن البين بنفسه إذا كان أنه لا شيء من المتقابلات العلم به واحدًا فيؤخذ بدل هذا عكسه لم يذعن له الذهن إذعانه لهذا بل احتاج أن يذكر أن السالبة نتعكس فيحتاج أن يريد في الكلام ما عنه غنى ونحن وإن قلنا إن السالبة الكلية تنعكس مثل نفسها فلم نضمن أن عكسها مثلها في البيان. ويجب أن نتأمل في المقاومة ما يوجبه الرأي المحمود فتؤخذ المقاومة مقدمة
Section: On the Syllogism of Resistance. As for the syllogism of resistance, it is a syllogism composed of two premises producing a conclusion opposite to a premise that is in another syllogism. Since it produces an opposite to a premise of that syllogism, it invalidates it. So the purpose of resistance is to invalidate the universal premise in the syllogism, for it is the most evident part of the syllogism. And that is in two ways: either by obstinacy (inad) or by contradiction (munaqadah). [A] As for obstinacy, it is that you make the major premise in the syllogism with which you oppose the universal premise more general in scope than that premise and opposite in quality. For example, if the ruling in the first premise is about something general like 'opposites', and the ruling on it is that knowledge of them is one, we come as the resistor and take what is more general than 'opposites' and rule on it with the opposite of that ruling, namely we deny that ruling universally, saying 'No correlatives have knowledge of them as one', and we add our statement 'Opposites are correlatives', to produce the opposite of the mentioned premise. Then this syllogism, if it produces the opposite of the major of the first syllogism and the minor of the first syllogism is added to it, it produces the opposite of the desired conclusion of the first syllogism. For example, the first syllogism is our statement 'B is J' and 'Every J is A' producing 'B is A'. It is objected that 'B is not A', because 'B is J' and 'No J is A' produce 'B is not A', which is the opposite of the conclusion of the first syllogism. [B] As for contradiction, it is that the claim in the first syllogism is universal, so the resistor brings a particular of the subject that does not have the mentioned ruling, and that particular is noted first when looking for contradiction, as the subject for the two terms of contradiction. This form is not the form of the first figure nor the second figure. So every positive resistance is either in the first figure or the third figure. As for the second figure, a positive resistance is impossible. As for the negative, it is not evident except that the speech in the major indicates the aspect of resistance, namely that its subject is exactly the subject of the resistance. For example, we say 'Opposites are correlatives, and nothing that has one knowledge is a correlative'—then the indication of self-evidence is considered to prove it by conversion. For if it is self-evident that no correlatives have one knowledge, and we take its converse instead, the mind would not assent to it as it does to this, but would need to mention that the negative converts—so the speech becomes redundant. And even if we say that the universal negative converts as itself, we do not guarantee that its converse is equal to it in evidence. And one must reflect on resistance as sound opinion requires, so that the resistance is taken as a premise.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the 'syllogism of resistance' (qiyas al-muqawamah), a dialectical tool used to refute a premise of another syllogism. The author begins by stating that muqawamah is a syllogism of two premises that produces a conclusion opposite to a premise in another syllogism, thereby invalidating it. Its purpose is to attack the universal premise (often the major) of the original syllogism, which is the most evident part. There are two types: inad (obstinacy) and munaqadah (contradiction). In inad, the resistor selects a term more general than the subject of the target premise and makes a statement of opposite quality (affirmative vs. negative). An example is given: the original premise 'Every opposite has one knowledge' (or more precisely, the judgment that knowledge of opposites is one); the resistor takes 'correlatives' (more general than 'opposites') and says 'No correlative has one knowledge', then adds 'Every opposite is a correlative' to conclude the opposite of the original premise. This counter-syllogism, when combined with the minor of the original, yields a conclusion opposite to the original conclusion. The author then illustrates with symbolic letters: the original 'B is J, every J is A, therefore B is A' is opposed by 'B is J, no J is A, therefore B is not A'. In munaqadah, the resistor presents a particular instance of the subject that does not bear the universal judgment. The form of this resistance is not that of the first or second figure; the author later clarifies that positive muqawamah occurs only in the first or third figures, never in the second. The passage then discusses negative muqawamah, noting that it is not self-evident unless the major's subject is exactly the subject of the resistance. An example is given: 'Opposites are correlatives; nothing that has one knowledge is a correlative'—but this conclusion is not immediate; one might think to convert the negative premise, but the author warns that conversion of a negative does not carry the same evidence. The passage ends by urging careful reflection to follow sound opinion when constructing a muqawamah. This is a detailed expansion on a dialectical concept, typical of a commentary (sharh) that probes and justifies the matn claim.

عكس
بيان
المقاومة السالبة

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
Muqawama: Constructing a Syllogistic Opposition
Author explains how to construct a muqawama opposition using a broader term and opposite judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a muqawama opposition by taking a broader term and applying the opposite judgment to counter a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Muqawama defeats a syllogism by using a broader term with the opposite judgment.

When facing a syllogism that seemingly proves a conclusion, you are not without recourse. The ancient logicians developed a technique called muqawama (constructive opposition) that allows you to build a counter-syllogism. By selecting a term more general than the subject of the original premise and applying a universal negative judgment, you can produce a conclusion that directly contradicts the original. This lesson unpacks the method with a concrete example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism's major premise
Find the universal affirmative premise in the original argument. For example, 'Every contrary is such that knowledge of it is one.'
This premise is the target.
2
Determine a broader term that includes the subject
Choose a term more general than the subject of that premise. If the subject is 'contraries', a broader term could be 'correlates' (which includes contraries).
The broader term must truly encompass the original subject.
3
Apply the opposite judgment to that broader term
Change the universal affirmative to a universal negative. So instead of 'Every correlate is such that knowledge of it is one', we assert 'No correlate is such that knowledge of it is one.'
This is the new major premise.
4
Construct the counter-syllogism
Combine the new major premise with the original minor premise (e.g., 'All contraries are correlates') to produce a conclusion: 'Therefore, no contrary is such that knowledge of it is one.'
This conclusion is the opposite of the original conclusion.
5
Verify the counter-conclusion
Check that the new conclusion indeed contradicts the original conclusion. If the original said 'some contraries are such that knowledge is one', the new universal negative is a stronger opposition.
This method works only if the broader term is genuinely wider and the minor premise holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدّمة الأولى على شيء عام كالأضداد، ويكون الحكم عليها أن العلم بها واحد فنجيء المقاوم ونأخذ ما هو أعم من الأضداد ونحكم عليه بضد الحكم الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو أن يسلب عنه ذلك الحكم سلبًا كليا فنقول ولا شيء من المتقابلات يكون العلم بها واحدًا، ونضيف إليه قولنا إن المتضادات متقابلات لينتج مقابل المقدّمة المذكورة. ثمَّ هذا القياس إذا كان منتجا ما يقابل كبرى القياس الأول وتضاف إليه الصغرى من القياس الأول ينتج مقابل النتيجة المطلوبة بالقياس الأول مثلا القياس الأول قولنا «بج» وكل «ج أ» ينتجان ب أ)، فعُورض أن «ب» ليس «أ» لأن (ب) ج» ولا شيء من ج أ» ينتجان «ب» ليس الذي هو مقابل نتيجة القياس الأول. [ب] وأما المناقضة فهي أن تكون الدعوى في القياس الأول كليًّا فيورد المقاوم جزئيا من الموضوع ليس فيه الحكم المذكور، ويكون ذلك الجزئي ملحوظا إليه أول ما يلحظ للمناقضة على أنه موضوع لطرفي المناقضة؛ وهذه الهيئة ليست هيئة الشكل الأول ولا الشكل الثاني. فإذن كل مقاومة موجبة؛ فإما في الشكل الأول، وإما في الشكل الثالث. وأما في الشكل الثاني فلا يمكن مقاومة موجبة. وأما السالبة فلا تبين به إلا وأن يعبر الكلام في الكبرى عن وجه المقاومة وهو أن يكون موضوعه هو بعينه موضوع المقاومة مثلا نقول إن الأضداد متقابلة ولا شيء مما به العلم واحد بمقابل فيعتبر الدلالة على البيان بنفسه إلى إثباته بالعكس فإن البين بنفسه إذا كان أنه لا شيء من المتقابلات العلم به واحدًا فيؤخذ بدل هذا عكسه لم يذعن له الذهن إذعانه لهذا بل احتاج أن يذكر أن السالبة نتعكس فيحتاج أن يريد في الكلام ما عنه غنى ونحن وإن قلنا إن السالبة الكلية تنعكس مثل نفسها فلم نضمن أن عكسها مثلها في البيان. ويجب أن نتأمل في المقاومة ما يوجبه الرأي المحمود فتؤخذ المقاومة مقدمة محمودة؛ وإن لم تكن أوليته يستعان فيها أيضًا بالقياس الاستثنائي مثلا أنه لو كان العلم بالأضداد واحدًا لكان المعلوم والمجهول يقع عليهما علم واحد، وكذلك النظر في الأضداد. والنظر في الاشتباه كقولهم لو كان العلم بالمضافين واحدًا لكان العدم والملكة واحدا، وهنا أشياء أخرى من هذا الا الباب تعرف من بعد في الجدل.
The first premise about a general thing like contraries, and the judgment about it is that knowledge of them is one. So we come with the opponent and take what is more general than the contraries and judge on it with the opposite of the judgment mentioned, which is to divest it of that judgment universally, and we say: No thing of the correlates is such that knowledge of it is one. And we add to it our statement: Contraries are correlates, to produce the opposite of the mentioned premise. Then this syllogism, if it produces what is opposite of the major of the first syllogism, and the minor of the first syllogism is added to it, produces the opposite of the desired conclusion of the first syllogism. For example, the first syllogism is our statement: 'B is J' and 'Every J is A', they produce 'B is A'. So it is objected that 'B is not A' because 'B is J' and 'No J is A' produce 'B is not', which is the opposite of the conclusion of the first syllogism. As for contradiction (munaqada), it is that the claim in the first syllogism is universal, so the opponent brings a particular of the subject in which the mentioned judgment is not present, and that particular is regarded first as subject for the two sides of the contradiction; and this form is not the form of the first figure nor the second figure. So every affirmative opposition is either in the first figure or the third figure. As for the second figure, affirmative opposition is not possible. As for the negative, it is not clarified except that the speech in the major expresses the aspect of opposition, which is that its subject is exactly the subject of the opposition. For example, we say: Contraries are correlates, and nothing of that whose knowledge is one is a correlate. So the indication of self-evidence is considered by itself to its proof by conversion, for the self-evident, if it is that no thing of the correlates is such that knowledge of it is one, and its converse is taken instead, the mind does not assent to it as it assents to this, rather it needs to mention that the negative is converted, so it needs to intend in speech what is unnecessary. And although we said that the universal negative converts like itself, we did not guarantee that its converse is like it in self-evidence. And we must examine in opposition what good opinion necessitates, so the opposition is taken as an accepted premise; even if it is not self-evident, help is also sought in it by conditional syllogism, for example, that if knowledge of contraries were one, then the known and the unknown would be covered by one knowledge. And similarly with consideration of contraries. And the consideration of ambiguity like their statement: If knowledge of correlatives were one, then non-existence and possession would be one. And there are other things of this chapter that are known later in dialectic.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage discusses two methods of opposing a syllogism: muqawama (constructive opposition) and munaqada (destructive contradiction). The author explains how to construct an opposing syllogism by taking a more general term than the subject of the original premise and applying the opposite judgment. For example, if the original premise is 'Contraries are such that knowledge of them is one', the opponent takes 'correlates' (which includes contraries) and judges that no correlates are such that knowledge is one. This yields the opposite conclusion. The passage then defines munaqada as bringing a particular instance from the universal claim that lacks the attributed judgment, and notes that this form does not correspond to the first or second figure. The author clarifies that affirmative opposition is possible only in the first and third figures, while negative opposition requires careful treatment of the major premise. He emphasizes that conversion of a universal negative does not guarantee equal self-evidence. The passage ends with examples using conditional syllogisms and mentions that more will be covered in dialectic.

Key Terms
المقاومة
الشكل
المتضادات
المتقابلات

Checkpoint
In muqawama, if the original premise is 'Every intoxicant is forbidden', what broader term could the opponent take and what judgment must he apply?
A) 'Every drink that causes drowsiness' with 'No drink that causes drowsiness is forbidden'
B) 'Every drink that causes drowsiness' with 'Every drink that causes drowsiness is forbidden'
C) 'Every intoxicant' with 'No intoxicant is forbidden'
D) 'Every forbidden thing' with 'No forbidden thing is an intoxicant'

Key Takeaway
Muqawama is a powerful tool to rebut a syllogism by substituting the major premise with its opposite using a broader term.

Munaqada and the Limits of Syllogistic Opposition
Author defines munaqada as a particular counterexample and discusses figure constraints and conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a munaqada opposition and explain why it does not conform to the first or second syllogistic figures.

Core Idea
Munaqada attacks a universal claim by citing a particular instance that lacks the judgment.

Not all opposition to a syllogism follows a clean syllogistic form. The author introduces munaqada, a method where the objector brings a particular counterexample from within the subject of a universal claim. This tactic does not fit the patterns of the first or second figure, and the author clarifies that affirmative opposition can only occur in the first and third figures. This lesson dissects these constraints and the nuances of negative opposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal claim being opposed
Find the universal premise in the original argument, e.g., 'Every contrary is such that knowledge of it is one.'
The claim must be universal for munaqada to work.
2
Find a particular instance (جزئي) of the subject that lacks the predicate
The opponent selects one specific member of the subject class that does not have the attributed property. For example, a particular pair of contraries where knowledge is not one.
This is a counterexample to the universal claim.
3
Recognize that this form does not fit figures 1 or 2
The author states that munaqada is not in the form of the first or second figure. It is a direct contradiction rather than a syllogistic deduction.
The objector is not constructing a full syllogism but a simple opposition.
4
Understand the figure constraints for affirmative opposition
The author asserts that every affirmative opposition (muqawama or munaqada) must be in the first or third figure; the second figure cannot have affirmative opposition.
This is a structural rule of categorical syllogisms.
5
Consider the conversion issue for negative opposition
For negative opposition, the major premise must have its subject exactly as the subject of the opposition. Conversion of a universal negative may not be equally self-evident.
The author warns that conversion does not preserve self-evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدّمة الأولى على شيء عام كالأضداد، ويكون الحكم عليها أن العلم بها واحد فنجيء المقاوم ونأخذ ما هو أعم من الأضداد ونحكم عليه بضد الحكم الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو أن يسلب عنه ذلك الحكم سلبًا كليا فنقول ولا شيء من المتقابلات يكون العلم بها واحدًا، ونضيف إليه قولنا إن المتضادات متقابلات لينتج مقابل المقدّمة المذكورة. ثمَّ هذا القياس إذا كان منتجا ما يقابل كبرى القياس الأول وتضاف إليه الصغرى من القياس الأول ينتج مقابل النتيجة المطلوبة بالقياس الأول مثلا القياس الأول قولنا «بج» وكل «ج أ» ينتجان ب أ)، فعُورض أن «ب» ليس «أ» لأن (ب) ج» ولا شيء من ج أ» ينتجان «ب» ليس الذي هو مقابل نتيجة القياس الأول. [ب] وأما المناقضة فهي أن تكون الدعوى في القياس الأول كليًّا فيورد المقاوم جزئيا من الموضوع ليس فيه الحكم المذكور، ويكون ذلك الجزئي ملحوظا إليه أول ما يلحظ للمناقضة على أنه موضوع لطرفي المناقضة؛ وهذه الهيئة ليست هيئة الشكل الأول ولا الشكل الثاني. فإذن كل مقاومة موجبة؛ فإما في الشكل الأول، وإما في الشكل الثالث. وأما في الشكل الثاني فلا يمكن مقاومة موجبة. وأما السالبة فلا تبين به إلا وأن يعبر الكلام في الكبرى عن وجه المقاومة وهو أن يكون موضوعه هو بعينه موضوع المقاومة مثلا نقول إن الأضداد متقابلة ولا شيء مما به العلم واحد بمقابل فيعتبر الدلالة على البيان بنفسه إلى إثباته بالعكس فإن البين بنفسه إذا كان أنه لا شيء من المتقابلات العلم به واحدًا فيؤخذ بدل هذا عكسه لم يذعن له الذهن إذعانه لهذا بل احتاج أن يذكر أن السالبة نتعكس فيحتاج أن يريد في الكلام ما عنه غنى ونحن وإن قلنا إن السالبة الكلية تنعكس مثل نفسها فلم نضمن أن عكسها مثلها في البيان. ويجب أن نتأمل في المقاومة ما يوجبه الرأي المحمود فتؤخذ المقاومة مقدمة محمودة؛ وإن لم تكن أوليته يستعان فيها أيضًا بالقياس الاستثنائي مثلا أنه لو كان العلم بالأضداد واحدًا لكان المعلوم والمجهول يقع عليهما علم واحد، وكذلك النظر في الأضداد. والنظر في الاشتباه كقولهم لو كان العلم بالمضافين واحدًا لكان العدم والملكة واحدا، وهنا أشياء أخرى من هذا الا الباب تعرف من بعد في الجدل.
The first premise about a general thing like contraries, and the judgment about it is that knowledge of them is one. So we come with the opponent and take what is more general than the contraries and judge on it with the opposite of the judgment mentioned, which is to divest it of that judgment universally, and we say: No thing of the correlates is such that knowledge of it is one. And we add to it our statement: Contraries are correlates, to produce the opposite of the mentioned premise. Then this syllogism, if it produces what is opposite of the major of the first syllogism, and the minor of the first syllogism is added to it, produces the opposite of the desired conclusion of the first syllogism. For example, the first syllogism is our statement: 'B is J' and 'Every J is A', they produce 'B is A'. So it is objected that 'B is not A' because 'B is J' and 'No J is A' produce 'B is not', which is the opposite of the conclusion of the first syllogism. As for contradiction (munaqada), it is that the claim in the first syllogism is universal, so the opponent brings a particular of the subject in which the mentioned judgment is not present, and that particular is regarded first as subject for the two sides of the contradiction; and this form is not the form of the first figure nor the second figure. So every affirmative opposition is either in the first figure or the third figure. As for the second figure, affirmative opposition is not possible. As for the negative, it is not clarified except that the speech in the major expresses the aspect of opposition, which is that its subject is exactly the subject of the opposition. For example, we say: Contraries are correlates, and nothing of that whose knowledge is one is a correlate. So the indication of self-evidence is considered by itself to its proof by conversion, for the self-evident, if it is that no thing of the correlates is such that knowledge of it is one, and its converse is taken instead, the mind does not assent to it as it assents to this, rather it needs to mention that the negative is converted, so it needs to intend in speech what is unnecessary. And although we said that the universal negative converts like itself, we did not guarantee that its converse is like it in self-evidence. And we must examine in opposition what good opinion necessitates, so the opposition is taken as an accepted premise; even if it is not self-evident, help is also sought in it by conditional syllogism, for example, that if knowledge of contraries were one, then the known and the unknown would be covered by one knowledge. And similarly with consideration of contraries. And the consideration of ambiguity like their statement: If knowledge of correlatives were one, then non-existence and possession would be one. And there are other things of this chapter that are known later in dialectic.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage discusses two methods of opposing a syllogism: muqawama (constructive opposition) and munaqada (destructive contradiction). The author explains how to construct an opposing syllogism by taking a more general term than the subject of the original premise and applying the opposite judgment. For example, if the original premise is 'Contraries are such that knowledge of them is one', the opponent takes 'correlates' (which includes contraries) and judges that no correlates are such that knowledge is one. This yields the opposite conclusion. The passage then defines munaqada as bringing a particular instance from the universal claim that lacks the attributed judgment, and notes that this form does not correspond to the first or second figure. The author clarifies that affirmative opposition is possible only in the first and third figures, while negative opposition requires careful treatment of the major premise. He emphasizes that conversion of a universal negative does not guarantee equal self-evidence. The passage ends with examples using conditional syllogisms and mentions that more will be covered in dialectic.

Key Terms
المناقضة
الشكل الأول
الشكل الثاني
الشكل الثالث
السالب الكلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which figures is affirmative opposition possible?
A) First and second figures
B) First and third figures
C) Second and third figures
D) Only the first figure

Key Takeaway
Munaqada is a dialectical move that contradicts a universal claim with a particular instance and does not follow standard syllogistic figures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the universal claim 'Every act that is mubah is permissible but not rewarded'. Using munaqada, find a particular action that is mubah but is rewarded (e.g., eating with the intention of gaining strength for worship). Explain how this particular contradicts the universal claim and why this form is not a standard syllogistic figure.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Qiyas al-Dalil: Syllogism of Proof with an Accepted Major
Definition of qiyas al-dalil, its components, and contrast with natural indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a qiyas al-dalil and distinguish it from natural indication.

Core Idea
In qiyas al-dalil, the major premise is an accepted opinion, not a necessary truth.

This lesson introduces the first type of syllogism used in rhetorical argument: qiyas al-dalil, or the syllogism of proof. Unlike a natural sign (like smoke for fire), the proof here relies on the entire proposition being accepted by the public as a given. Understanding this helps you see how arguments in Islamic discourse often appeal to commonly held beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise as accepted
In qiyas al-dalil, the major premise is described as muḥmūda, i.e., an accepted opinion that the public acknowledges. It is not a necessary truth or a revealed text, but a widely held belief.
Example: 'Envious people are hated' is an accepted opinion.
2
Recognize the minor as a particular statement
The minor premise is a particular statement about an individual, like 'So-and-so is envious.' This applies the general accepted rule to a specific case.
3
Understand the proof as the whole syllogism
The proof is not that one part indicates another (like smoke indicates fire), but that the entire syllogism (both premises together) yields a conclusion that is acknowledged by the public.
The author contrasts this with natural indication to clarify the distinction.
4
Note the temporal scope and potentiality
The syllogism can address future, present, general, or most cases. If the major is only potential (not explicitly stated), the syllogism becomes a 'syllogism of conscience' (qiyas al-ḍamīr).
This leads into more advanced topics covered later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في [ قياس ] الدليل [ وقياس ] العلامة
أما الدليل فهو قياس مؤلف من مقدمتين كبراه مقدمة محمودة؛ يراها الجمهور ويقول بها؛ وتؤخذ حجة ودليلا لا على سبيل أن جزءا منه يدل على جزء آخر مثل الدخان على النار بل على أن نفس القول الحاصل من الجزأين معترف به. وربَّما كان على أمر مستقبل، وربَّما كان على أمر حاضر، وربَّما كان عاما، وربَّما كان على الأكثر مثل قولهم إن الحساد ممقوتون، والمنعمون مودودون»، فإنها بين المقدمتين دليلان أو يتحد منهما الدليل على أنها تجعل كبرى بالقوة أو بالفعل؛ وليس الغرض أن نفس المقت أو الود علامة ودليل على أن هذا بنفسه دليل أي متَّبع مقبول مرجوع البتة: فإن الدليل في هذا الموضع يراد به هذا وهذه المقدّمات تؤخذ في القياس من الكبريات وصغرياتها شخصيات كقولهم إن فلاناً حسود وإن فلانًا منعم»، وحمل هذه المقدمات بحسب الظن الغالب. والكبريات إذا كانت بالقوة كان القياس نوعًا من قياس الضمير ؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد. وقياس العلامة أيضًا يرجع إلى الأشكال، وإنه ضمير يثبت فيه الأكبر للأصغر بعلامة. وتلك العلامة إما ضرورية وإما محمودة مظنونة فهو؛ والحد الأوسط في هذا القياس يقع على ثلاث جهات وذلك لأن الأوسط [1] إما أن يكون محمولا على الأصغر دون الأكبر مثل اللبن إذا جعل علامة للولادة فيقال هذه المرأة لها لبن فقد ولدت
Chapter: On the syllogism of proof and the syllogism of the sign. As for the proof, it is a syllogism composed of two premises, its major being an accepted premise, which the public sees and affirms; and it is taken as an argument and proof, not in the way that a part of it indicates another part like smoke indicates fire, but rather in the way that the very statement resulting from the two parts is acknowledged. And sometimes it concerns a future matter, sometimes a present matter, sometimes general, sometimes for the most part, like their statement: 'Envious people are hated, and benefactors are loved.' For between the two premises there are two proofs, or the proof from them is one, so that they are made major potentially or actually. And the intent is not that the hatred or love itself is a sign and proof that this itself is a proof, i.e., followed, accepted, fully reliable. For proof in this place is intended as such. And these premises are taken in the syllogism from the majors and their minors are particular statements, like their statement: 'So-and-so is envious' and 'So-and-so is a benefactor.' And the carrying of these premises is according to predominant opinion. And if the majors are potential, the syllogism is a kind of syllogism of the conscience (qiyas al-damir), and that will be known later. The syllogism of the sign also goes back to the figures, and it is a syllogism of the conscience in which the major is asserted for the minor by a sign. And that sign is either necessary or an accepted opinion. And the middle term in this syllogism occurs in three ways: because the middle [1] is either predicated of the minor but not of the major, like milk if made a sign of childbirth, so it is said: This woman has milk, so she has given birth.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage introduces two types of syllogisms used in rhetorical argument: qiyas al-dalil (syllogism of proof) and qiyas al-'alama (syllogism of sign). The author defines the first as a syllogism whose major premise is an accepted opinion (muhmuda) that the public agrees upon. Unlike a natural indication (like smoke indicating fire), the proof here relies on the entire proposition being accepted. It can be about future, present, general, or most cases, e.g., 'Envious people are hated, benefactors are loved.' The minors are particular statements about individuals. If the majors are only potential, it becomes a syllogism of conscience (qiyas al-damir). The second type, qiyas al-'alama, uses a sign to affirm the major for the minor. The sign can be necessary or accepted opinion. The middle term in this syllogism has three placements: either predicated of the minor but not the major (e.g., milk as a sign of childbirth), or predicated of the major but not the minor, or both. The example given is the first placement: 'This woman has milk, so she has given birth.'

Key Terms
الدليل
المقدمة المحمودة
قياس الضمير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In qiyas al-dalil, the major premise is described as muḥmūda (accepted). Which of the following best exemplifies a muḥmūda premise?
A) Every intoxicant is forbidden (based on Qur'anic text).
B) Envious people are hated (a commonly accepted opinion).
C) Fire is hot (a necessary truth).
D) The world is temporally originated (a philosophical assertion).

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-dalil is a syllogism whose major is an accepted opinion, used to produce rhetorical proof by appealing to common belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a qiyas al-dalil using a commonly accepted opinion (e.g., 'Oppressors are condemned by society') and a particular instance (e.g., 'Zaid is an oppressor'), and write the full syllogism.
?
Identify the major premise in the following argument as muḥmūda or not: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (based on Qur'an); this nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore it is forbidden.' Explain why.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
Qiyas al-'Alama: Syllogism of Sign with Middle Term Placements
Definition of qiyas al-'alama, its middle term as a sign, and its three placements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three placements of the middle term in qiyas al-'alama and give examples from Islamic reasoning.

Core Idea
In the syllogism of sign, the middle term is a sign that links the minor and major, either necessarily or by accepted opinion.

This lesson explores qiyas al-'alama, the syllogism of sign, where the middle term serves as a sign indicating the major for the minor. The sign can be necessary (like milk indicating childbirth) or based on accepted opinion. Understanding the three placements of the middle term is crucial for analyzing arguments in medicine, fiqh, and other fields.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the middle term as a sign
In qiyas al-'alama, the middle term is called a sign ('alāma). It is used to prove that the major property belongs to the minor subject. The sign itself can be necessary (like physical evidence) or accepted by opinion.
Example: milk is a sign of childbirth in women.
2
Learn the first placement: middle predicated of minor but not major
The author gives an example: 'This woman has milk (middle), so she has given birth (major).' Here, milk (middle) is predicated of the woman (minor), but not predicated of giving birth. This is the only placement explicitly mentioned in the passage.
The other two placements are implied: middle predicated of major but not minor, and middle predicated of both.
3
Understand the sign's modality
The sign can be either necessary (ḍarūrī) or based on accepted opinion (maḥmūda maẓnūna). This affects the certainty of the conclusion.
Necessary sign leads to a certain conclusion; accepted opinion leads to a probable one.
4
Relate to syllogistic figures
The author notes that this syllogism also goes back to the figures (ashkāl). The middle term's placement determines which figure the syllogism belongs to.
This connects to the general theory of categorical syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
